to be addressed 10-William M'ageeod; Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed to J. F.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and', not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs; Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin” will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do notwish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, JUNE 20, 1891.
An Australian Paper Currency.
The failure of the three last Australian
loans seems to suggest that the great days of
the borrowing industry are about over. The
collapse—if it is a collapse—has come with
surprising suddenness, and why it has come
no one seems to know. Possibly the British
lender has at last begun to realise that at
least three-fourths of the loans he has scat-
tered over all parts of the A\ T orld will never
come back—that every state on earth is
engaged in the borrowing industry, and
that, with one exception, none of them seem
even to dream of repayment—and that a
nation Avhich always borrows and never pays
is going exactly the same road as a man who
floats a neiv I O U every day, and never, by
any chance, settles up. Probably, he wants
digger percentage and more pickings, and
if so it is devoutly to be hoped that he may
not get them, for the percentage of the past
has been sufficiently ruinous without any
enlargement. Or, it may be, the stoppage
is only a temporary one, and that in a few
months more Australia’s promise to pay may
agjiin be acceptable tender in the money-
market of London, but this last and worst
disaster, it is to.be trusted, will not come to
pass. For the Australian people have already
lost enough by that great influx of foreign
capital which is fooled away in buying up
land for railway purposes at eleven times its
market value, and thus creating a race of
capitalists Avho go ,t° England with their
plunder and lie about this countrv at
greasy public feeds ; and if the great
borrowing industry can be brought up with
a shock it will be a blessing of no ordinary
character. But if, through his own sinful-
ness, or the wrath of Providence, or any
other cause, the British financier should be
willing, either now or at any future time, to
lend more money to this unhappy country,
it is worth considering whether the nation
would not do well to pass a law for the sum-
mary execution of any statesman who
attempts to float another loan in any foreign
market. And, having done this, the country
might sober up to some extent and forswear
the curse of imported capital so far as is pos-
sible, and fill the void—in a measure, at
least—with a Government paper-currency—
that old and valuable expedient which is
cursed by every banker on this earth,
because the banker lives to a great extent by
issuing a very questionable and shaky paper-
currency of his own, and ho feels bad in the
inmost parts of his soul when the State
threatens to interfere with his fat monopoly.
* * #
Paper-money is an essential part of the
national currency. It must be issued either
by the State or by some private institution,
for it has become absolutely indispensable.
The institution which issues it supplies an
urgent need, and while thus benefiting the
nation it also benefits itself, for it virtually
borrows the public money without paying
any interest. Yet, for some reason which
never will be accurately explained, the Aus-
tralian Governments prefer to do all their
borrowing in the London market at 3| per
cent,, while the privilege of raising money
without interest is conferred upon various
financial institutions, which make a large
part of their revenue by fooling with the
currency. This proceeding is based strictly
upon the laws of political economy, for that
science was fixed up to a large “extent by
bankers and financiers, who fixed it in the
way that was best adapted to boom their
own commissions and percentages. Political
economy is the cause and basis of Freetrade,
and the prop and stay of the alleged law of
supply and demand—whereby the cheapest
only survives, and the great object of Trade
is to replace the sweated European at 3s.
a day by the sweated Chinaman at Is. fid.
per quarter. It is the support of private
property in land and of a thousand other
swindles, and in various unfathomable ways
it furnishes indisputable reasons why the
great banking monopoly must be preserved
for the safety and security of the nation ;
and yet, when all these things are burst
up, this lying science will be re-organised,
and its professors will contradict all their
cherished axioms of the past, and will declare
that they contradicted them all along,
* # *
The one essential of a paper currency is
that it must be convertible upon demand.
The paper issued by the banks is conver-
tible, in theory at least, and, therefore, it
maintains its value with reasonable success.
In reality, however, the Australian banks, like
most other institutions of their kind, would
close their doors if they were suddenly
called upon to act up to the promise that is
printed on the face of their notes, and redeem
their , outstanding liabilities immediately.
Their safety lies in this very danger ; because
when notes are convertible there is never,
except in times of panic, any attempt to
convert them. Therefore, these institutions
trade joyously on the public money, and
gradually progress till they have a lien over
all Australasia, and they buy up the nation
with its own cash, and lend to it at high
interest the same funds which they borrowed
from it without any interest at all. Yet the
currency is obviously a matter for which the
State alone should be responsible, and the
Government which permits any practically
irresponsible institution to plunder the
people for its own benefit by the issue of
paper-money, with the further chance that
in some great day of commercial disaster the
said money will become saleable only by the
ton, is guilty of gross and infamous neglect.
If the future Commonwealth would take
this matter into its own hands, and issue a
redeemable paper currency of its own by the
medium of a State Bank, it would thus ob-
tain without interest an immediate loan
from the nation of at least £5,000,000, equal
to an annual revenue of some £300,000, and
as the population increased, and the demand
for money became greater, the amount of
this annual income would be steadily en-
larged. Out of this fund the public works
policy could be continued, much less rapidly
it is true, than heretofore, but without any
further addition to the public burdens, and
as the works thus constructed became more
and more profitable in the course of yeai’s,
progress would be accelerated in a steadily-
increasing ratio. And so far as concerns the
guarantee against depreciation of the national
securities, the note-holders would be pro-
tected in exactly the same manner as at pre-
sent. Their paper would be convertible on
demand, and the reserve of gold in the State
Bank would be sufficient to convert any
quantity of notes that were likely to
be presented. If, by any imaginable
contingency, the whole, or even a large pro-
portion of the notes, could be pi’esented in
one mass, the State Bank certainly could not
convert them, and in that case it would do
exactly what the existing banks would do
under similar conditions —suspend payment.
In the early days of the Bank of Exxgland, a
body of enterprising Jacobites bought up an
enormous quantity of notes and suddenly
dumping them down on the counter de-
manded gold in exchange, and on that
memorable occasion the institution saved its
credit by the simple process of ejecting, the
note-holders froxii the premises. Should a
State Bank be established in Australia it is
more than probable that an attempt would
be made by the present banking monopolists
to wreck it in similar fashion, and in that
event a somewhat similar device might come
in useful. Or, a more lawful proceeding,
the enterprising speculators might get three
years hard labour for conspiracy, intimida-
tion, illegal assembly, or some similar offence
which might fit the case.
# * *
A vast amount of drivel has been spoken
and written against the expedient of a State
Bank of issue, and many woeful illustrations
have been presented to show the evils that
accrue from such a scheme, but the illustra-
tions are all iri’elevant, and the. drivel has
no bearing on the case. The French
Directorate poured out depreciated assignats
until their value fell so low that a note of the
nominal value of 20 francs would barely fetch
enough to pay for paper and printing, and
the American greenbacks threatened, at one
time, to sink to the same level ; but these
and a hundred similar issues were irredeem-
able currencies, and had no more to do with
the case than has the depreciation of a pile
of wild-cat silver scrip. The question at
issue is not one of flooding the country with
paper bearing a forced and entirely artificial
value ; it is nob-suggested that the Govern-
ment should resort to the cheap and Simple
but ruinous device of printing a million
pounds worth of money wheneveiv a million
pounds is wanted in the Treasury. \lf such a
scheme were feasible no Government need
ever go bankrupt so long as the paper and
ink held out and the printing machine was in
working order* But the problem to be con-
sidered is whether the Government should
not supply by means of a State Bank the
demand which is now supplied by other in-
stitutions, and thus secure for the nation the
immense profits which now. go to pay divi-
dends to a small ring of monopolists—•
whether it should borrow money at
high interest in England while allowing
sundry capitalists a privilege by which they
are enabled to borrow money gratis in
Australia—and whether there is any more
sense or reason in allowing the paper-
currency of the country to be in the hands
of rings and syndicates than in allowing
these same syndicates to coin gold and
silver? For under present conditions the
greater part of the money in circulation has
no value at all except such as it acquires
through the solvency of certain private
speculators, and the Government has given
away a great source of revenue and got
nothing but public insecurity in return.
And, considering how much insecurity there
is lying about loose in all parts of the world,
and how cheaply it can be acquired, it seems,
in this case, as if the article had been bought
much too dear. The subject is one which
has never been put fairly before the public,
for political economy is a lopsided and lying
science which is run chiefly in the interests •
of syndicates and capitalists. Even an
irredeemable paper-currency-—though it has
no more to do with the question at issue
than Hamlet had with the Schleswig-
Holstein difficulty—is not necessarily a
greater evil than the present system may,
under certain circumstances, prove to be.
The American greenback sank sufficiently
low in public estimation, yet the greenback,
in its worst days, was more valuable than
the notes of the most solvent bank in the
United States were in 1837—that famous
year when every bank in the Union stopped
payment, and every note in circulation
descended in one act to the value of a pipe-
light.
• # »
The arguments in favour of a State Bank
may thus be summed up in a few sentences.
By resuming the sole power of issuing bank-
notes, the Government would gain a large
revenue which it has hitherto given away to
a few monopolists without getting any ap-
preciable advantage in return. It would add
enormously to the security afforded co note-
holders. It would virtually borrow to all
eternity, increase steadily with the increase
of population, and out of this money—which
now goes to create dividends for the holders
of a fat monopoly—it could continue the
construction of public works without any
addition whatever to the public burdens. It
would do this, not by laying any new bur-
dens upon the people, but by supplying a
public want which it has hitherto left to be
supplied by syndicates and corporations ; and
as a result it would be enabled to put an end
to the nefarious system of borrowing which
threatens to make Australia the property of
a gang of foreign capitalists. And lastly,
the scheme involves no risk whatever of
flooding the country with that depreciated
paper which is the cause of so much ominous
foreboding among the banking fraternity,
for notes issued by a solvent institution and
redeemable on demand cannot depreciate by
any known possibility, and the Government
would, in this respect, occupy exactly the
same situation as the banks—its notes would
be redeemable, and for that very reason they
would never be redeemed. And against the
scheme there is no ai’gument whatever,
except the universal howl of the capitalistic
press, which always prophesies ruin and
universal chaos when another great and pro-
fitable monopoly starts on that downward
road that leads to Socialism and human
equality.
Equality of Representation.
“ O N ’E Man One Vote ”is all right so far as it
goes. We approve of it, of course, that goes
without saying; for every reader of The
Bulletin knows that it has consistently
written in support of the principle for years
past, and that The Bulletin was the first
paper in Australia to advocate it. One Man
One Vote will give equality of voting power;
hut, unfortunately, even that will not give
equality of representation. Let us look at the
matter as it affects New South Wales.
* * #
An analysis of the electoral rolls suggests
quite a number of questions ; such as the fol-
lowing Why should East Macquarie, with
2600 voters, and Glen limes, with 2699, have
two representatives each, while Shoalhaven,
with 2809, The Gwydir, with 2935, and Sturt,
with 8306, have but one each ? Why should
the Glebe, with 4752 electors, have but two
members, while Mudgee, with but 4083, is
allowed three? Why should 5923 voters be
allowed to outvote 8000, 9000, 10,000 and even
18,704 ? Taking it all round, this seems like a
reversal of the supposed central principle of
representative government, inasmuch as it is
more like “government by minorities ” than
“government by majorities.”
* * #
The nine great metropolitan and suburban
electorates returning four members each, are,
taking them in the order of their numerical
importance:—Canterbury, 18,704 ; West Syd-
uey, 10,821 ; Paddington, 10,397 ; East Sydney,
10,04:1 ; Redfern, 9960; Central Cumberland,
9914; South Sydney, 9862 ; Balmain, 9780 ;
and Newtown, 8163. These nine big electo-
rates, therefore, contain 97,642 voters, out of a
total of 304,648 for the whole province, or within
a trilie of one-third of the whole; while they
return but 36 representatives out of a total of
141, or but a shade over one-fourth. That,
however, is not the worst of it. Any one of
these great electorates can be outvoted by the
representatives of not more than 5923 voters.
Even Canterbury, with its great total of 18,704
voters, can be outvoted by the combined votes
of the representatives of Wilcannia, Went-
worth, Boorowa, West Macquarie, and Morpeth,
which return one member each ; live in all,
against Canterbury’s four. Yet these five places
total blit 6,923 votes, against Canterbury’s
18,704,
* * *
Nor does it even end here. The four largest,
numerically, of the metropolitan and suburban
electorates—Canterbury, West Sydney, Pad-
dington, East Sydney—with a total of 49,963
voters, can be outvoted by a combination con-
taining Mudgee, The Murray, Argyle, East
Maitland, Glen limes, East Macquarie, Mor-
peth, West Macquarie, Wentworth, Boorowa,
and Wilcannia. These 11 electorates return 17
members, hence they are able to outvote the 16
representatives of the , four most populous met-
ropolitan and suburban electorates, but they
only contain 22,715 voters, or considerably less
than half the number contained in the four
electorates which they may outvote.
* * *
What becomes of “ government by majori-
ties ’’—nominal “Representative Government ”
—when 22,715 men can outvote 49,963? —when
5923 can outvote 18,704? —when 2600 (East
Macquarie, with two representatives) can out-
vote 8306 (Sturt, with but one’ representative) ?
What is the remedy ? Single electorates ? How
are you going to arrange your single electorates ?
On the basis of population ? Let us see how it
will work out. Total number of voters in New
South Wales, by latest returns, 304,648 ; num-
ber of representatives, 141. This gives one
representative to every 2160 (and a fraction.)
voters. No less than 22 single-member
electorates would be disfranchised, if this
were made the basis ; all the two-member
electorates—the Glebe only excepted—would be
reduced to one representative each ; four of the
three-member places would be reduced to two
each ; and Canterbury would have nine, and
West Sydney five members. The chief objec-
tion to this plan, of course, would be the total
disfranchisement of the 22 single-member
electorates. On the other hand, if the voting
power of the numerically smallest of these is to
be taken as the unit of representation, N.S.W.
would have an Assembly of 297 members; a
most absurdly excessive number. The only
way in which this could be avoided, without
disfranchising places now' represented, would be
by a complete and thorough re-mapping of the
electorates.
* * *
The only practicable escape from the double
dilemma of unequal representation, or over-
grown legislature, appears to be in making the
whole province one electorate ; but this should
undoubtedly be preceded by such a measure of
local self-government as should enable the
residents of all districts to attend to their own
requirements without reference to the general
legislature. When each county, municipality,
or other local Government was in a position to
provide for its own roads, bridges, and other
local requirements, there would no longer be
the necessity that there is now for returning
local representatives, since the mere “ roads -
and-bridges member ” would be distinctly out
of place in a Legislature that had no connection
with such matters, but attended solely to
national and international concerns. Hence it
would no longer be imperative on the residents
of Dead Dog Hollow to return to the
Legislative Assembly a representative who
w'ould support the construction of a
bridge over Dead Dog Creek at the expense of
the whole province ; but it w ould be distinctly
to the interest of the, inhabitants of each and
every portion of the province td obtain the ser-
vices of men capable of dealing with all vital
questions of general concern. Get rid of petty
local matters, and a Legislative* Assembly of 50
members could govern N.S. Wales better than
ever it has been governed by oile of 137 mem-
bers, or ever will be by 141,999, or any larger
number ; provided'that those 5() are elected bv
the manhood of the country, aiid not by mere
bricks, timber, mortar, and mud.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Our Kawlinies.”
This little paragraph, taken from the London
Hawk, is a fair sample of the hog-like bounce--
ableness of the modern Briton ;
It is lucky for us that such men as Johnnie Tools visit
our colonies; were it not so our cousins would hare a
poor idea of Englishmen of education in general and
actors in particular.
It is the terra “ onr colonies '’—that term which
glares out in every English newspaper from
Caithness to Land’s End—that amuses us.
Even the smallest kind of Englishman still
suffers from a vague and shapeless idea that he
owns the whole of Australasia—an idea that is
born of too much “ Rule Britannia ” and the
like unhealthy doggerel; and though he can’t
even raise a loaf on the security of his alleged
interest in this continent nothing ever seems to
break up his illusion. Yet England doesn’t
even own itself, and a nation that doesn’t own
itself assuredly can’t own anything else. A few
thousand landlords can give the whole English
people notice to quit at any moment, and
bounce the population of three kingdoms into
the sea, for the nation occupies its own country
solely by the landlord’s permission, and if it
suits him to fire it out it has simply got to go.
There may be no place for it to go to, but it
must go all the same, and leave behind it its
traditions, and its beloved sovereign, and the
spieling Tummy with his bagfull of gambling
plant, and its national anthem, and its glorious
memories of Cressy and Poictiers and
Agincourt and Trafalgar, and juU the rest of


the old warlike drivel of its ancestors. The
Englishman lives by exactly the same right of
tenure as a stray dog in a hogshead ; he doesn’t
own his country or his “ colonies,” or himself—
bis only property is the sole and undivided
light to move on, to the North Pole, or the
bottom of the German Ocean, or Hades, or
anywhere out of the way, when his owner
orders him to do so. He can’t even come to
his alleged kawlinies without permission, for
they are also owned by a batch of landlords ;
and not only the intruding Englishman, but
I lie Australian himself can be shifted right out
of the empire if the proprietors take the proper
legal steps to that effect. Therefore the
National Anthem, and the-“ Rule. Britannia,”,
and all the rest of the bumptious riot and
music are woefully misplaced ; they are
merely the whoopings of a lodger who
is cheering for his boarding-house, and
is liable to be- thrown down the steps
right in the middle of his enthusiasm. And he
doesn’t even own these patriotic songs, for they
arc part of the furniture of the boarding-house,
and so are the memories of Nelson and Wel-
lington, and the glories of Waterloo, and the
Charge of the Six Hundred, and everything
else that arouses the burning frenzy of the
British people. Now and then the super-
idiotic inhabitant goes out and hollers for this
furniture on some wild battlefield, and dies for
the fixtures and fittings of the national pre-
mises, but they belong to the landlord all the
same. The only difference in this matter be-
tween the Englishman and the Austral! in is
that the latter is sane enough to recognise that
lie doesn’t own Britain, while the former is mad
enough to think, without any greater vestige of
reason, that he does own Australia ; while in
reality the landlord owns both of them. He is
the only tiling connected with the whole show
that isn’t a delusion. The entire empire is a
fraud, for the landlord has legal authority to
fire out the population and leave nobody in the
empire except himself ; and the great, Imperial
army is an empty thing, for an army is no good
unless it has some place to stand upon, and the
landlord owns the place it stands upon ; and
even the capitalist—when he isn’t a landlord—
is hollow, for he has no single spot to lay down
his money upon except with the landlord’s per-
mission. And yet, in the face of all these facts,
the poor, wall-headed Briton still adheres to his
incurable delusion about his colonies ; and even
the obvious circumstance that he can’t stand on
any one of these colonies or on his own native
country cither, unless he gets permission to do
so and pays for the privilege, doesn’t seem to
explain the situation to him in the least.
Circus and Red Faint.
Brettv nearly all the cranks on earth seem to
be writing to the Argus just nowon the subject
of Federation. Most of them are writing about
the Commonwealth flag and the wattle, and the
bine Australian sky, and things like that, but
one advanced individual has another grievance.
In addition to the flag he wants this nation to
•adopt a symbol of some kind^an ivy leaf, he
thinks, would crowd up the yawning void as
well as anything—which symbol the inhabitants
of this country are to —
Wear on all occasions, in order 'that the manifestation
‘of a well-grounded enthusiasm may tend to assist the
‘deliberations of our legislators.
The Australian Natives’ Association crank
'sticks out conspicuously in this suggestion, and
unless the A.N.A. crank can be suppressed he
will end by making the Commonwealth ridicu-
lous. The Australian of the future is not going
to wear a vegetable on all occasions in order to
manifest enthusiasm, for no nation can enthuse
and whoop and pass resolutions all its life. The
A.N.A. has got an impression that when Aus-
tralia is federated the inhabitants are going to
•hurrah and let off rockets for ever afterwards,
but they are not —they will have to go out look-
ing for a job in the same sad, old way, and beer
•won’t be any cheaper, and rents will still go up,
and the drains will smell exactly the same, and
the European pauper will want to come here
.just as he does now. And the Federal legislator
won’t be “ assisted ’ in his deliberations by the
ipopulation wearing a leaf, any more than we
'will by the population wearing clothes or carry-
ing a saw—the kind of legislator who could bo
■assisted in any such way isn’t worth baying,
•anyhow ; and moreover it would be exceedingly
•rough on the Australian inhabitant, for he
anight lose his leaf when he was eleven miles
from home, and then he would have to walk
back and get another one, lest he should meet a
legislator, and the legislator should feel
Weakened and discouraged through seeing him
Avithout the symbolic herb. The A.N.A. has
fione a lot to create a national spirit in Aus-
tralia, and it would have been better if it had
left the job alone, for it has roused a lot of
tp&hriotic enthusiasm which isn’t worth having.
The details of Federation are left pretty
much to take care of themselves, and
while the imported groveller fixes up the
constitution of the Commonwealth in any way
that best suits his hunger after titles and mis-
cellaneous “honours,” the Australian “native
worries about flags and emblems and symbolic
vegetables, and makes weird whizzing noises
about his great continent, and barracks for the
cause of Unity, and devotes himself to rockets
and processions and other childlike lunacies. In
reality it doesn’t matter one solitary rap if
Australia never has a flag at all—a country can
pull through without a flag, and the sort of
patriotic fire that won’t blaze up except when
a garish rag is put up on a stick is exactly the
same sort of enthusiasm which is aroused bj
the sight of a cow with five legs, or a burst of
green and blue fireworks, and it isn’t worth
storage room. Also, the uniforms, and the
illuminations, and the processions, and the
drink, and the miscellaneous howling which
somehow grow up around every great national
movement are all absolutely useless —they are
not patriotism or devotion or any other thing
that is valued at one cent. But the typical
politician always encourages this mixture of
circus and red paint, because it diverts the
•attention of the nation and leaves him to fake
t he constitution in peace. He promotes banners,
and doggerel “National Anthems, and dis-
plays of troops, and the firing of big guns,
because they induce the people to mistake aim-
less whooping for liberty and equality, and it
is on this basis that the Commonwealth is likely
to be constructed. The imported placeman and
place-hunters are fixing up the foundations of
the new State, and the Native—that hunible
native who is always left out when the loaves
and fishes are going round—is uttering plati-
tudes, and barracking in the background, and
because he is allowed to barrack ho imagines
that he is running the country —but he isn’t.
He is only shaking a rattle, and banging a toy
drum, and making a show of himself,
”—-
Wanted, a Government Auditor.
The other day, in Melbourne, Mr. Edward
8000, who pleaded guilty to a £lO,OOO shortage
in his cash, was brought up for sentence, and
Pitrves, Q.C., arose, with tears in his voice,
and great hunks of pathos bursting out of him
in all directions, to whitewash his client, and
plead for a mitigation of sentence. And in the
course of about half-an-hour, he virtually proved
that Mr. 8000 was the only really straight man
going ; he was morally, if not legally, square ;
he was wool all through; and, in a general way,
he was a pillar for rectitude, and nothing but
the cussedness of the auditor was at fault:—
The main inducement to the crime was the extraordinary
want of supervision and carelessness of the directors,
manager, and auditors of the company. It excelled even
the Premier Permanent cases. Government would have
to appoint a Government director and a Government
auditor for each financial company, who would be entitled
to demand an audit at any time, and would he criminally
responsible for any neglect.
This was a remarkable utterance in its way,
because it was left unfinished. It ended just
there. There was no suggestion about arresting
the auditor for stupidity or neglect or on any
other charge, for somehow nobody ever does
arrest an auditor. Nearly every company in
Australia gets into trouble periodically because
somebody has been “faking ” the accounts and
the cash, and the blamed ass of an auditor
either is in the swim or has not had sense
enough to find it out : and the only remedy that
is ever proposed is to appoint an expensive
Government official—one separate Government
official to every company. The cost of such a
functionary would wreck any small enterprise,
and the chances are a hundred to one that he
would be a shade worse even than the unofficial
incubus now existing. And, moreover, if a
Government auditor could he made criminally
responsible, why not make the present auditor
criminally responsible in like manner ? When
some great institution breaks up, and £lOO,OOO
dissolves into thin air, the shareholders are
ruined because they paid an outside officer to
watch the principal hired swindlers , inside the
office, and he scooped in his salary under false
pretences ; therefore, he ought to be promptly
run in on the same criminal charge as the man
who raises the wind on a bogus cheque, or else
he should be tried for negligence and derelic-
tion of duty. His position is practically the
same as that of the individual who loses a
£lOO,OOO steamship, and the law jumps on him
remorselessly. No one can reach the man
whose foolishness has sunk a bank, so long as he
was only stupid and incompetent, but for
the man who has sunk a ship—even though no
lives have been lost and there is only the
financial aspect of the case to be considered —
stupidity and incompetence are actual crimes
and are rightly treated as such. The cause of
this difference is easily explained. The Legis-
latures of Australia are mostly made up of
financiers, speculators, directors, and company-
mongers, and most of these individuals are, or
have been, or will be at some future time,,
engaged in more or less questionable dodges,
with money entrusted to them by shareholders.
An auditor who was compelled, if only in order
to keep out of gaol, to be a reality and not a
sham, would furnish a snag on which half the
law-makers of this country would founder. The
proposition for the appointment of a race of
Government auditors implies that by no other
means can any individual plant his savings in a
bank or a building society without imminent
risk of being reduced to beggary, and it would
be equally reasonable to assume that no one
could safely go on board ship without a Govern-
ment official in command, or ride in a cab
without an expensive Civil-servant to drive.
And such a proposition, in the eloquent words
of Duncan Gillies —a man whose surpluses
were very much in want of auditing—is too
thin.
The Baccarat Case.
A jury has decided that Sir William Gordon-
Cumming cheated at baccarat, for the sufficient
reason that four people swore they saw him
cheat, and nobody but himself could possibly
swear to the contrary. The gallant- officer
was at a disadvantage which put him out of
Court before the case commenced. Any
witnesses for the defence would count for no
more than the folks who don’t see a conjuror
put the cannon-ball in the obliging gentleman’s
hat. CuMMiNtt’s counsel —who, by the way,
is a professional Tory and place-seeker —took
precious good care not to injure his own politi-
cal prospects by advancing the only arguments
likely to benefit his client. He feebly suggested
that the addle-pated snobs who saw the hanky-
panky perpetrated, again and again, mustall have
been mistaken. A smart young barrister with his
way to make, and no ‘ ‘ society ’ ’ standing to main-
tain, would have boldly suggested live things.
First, that the entire company, from the royal
banker downwards, were drunk and incap-
able of swearing to anything that occurred.
Secondly, that the oath of a woman in any
gambling transaction is incontrovertible evi-
dence to the opposite of what she states, and
that she cannot possibly speak the truth where
money is at stake. Thirdly, that Gumming
only did what he saw his accusers doing when
they got the chance, and therefore supposed
that his cheating was of a venial character and
made no difference to the “honour” of an
officer and a gentleman, which is elastic.
Fourthly, that the host and his family who
would, on their own showing, continue to
offer hospitality to a petty swindler (who had
the cheek to try and swindle such old case-
hardened roues and past-masters of gambling
as Wales and Williams by a most transparent
device under their very noses) and en-
courage him to play baccarat for the express
purpose of seeing him swindle under their own
roof—that such people were not good enough
to swear away the character of a bull-frog.
Fifthly—that the Wilson crowd, the Earl of
Coventry (Beerthirst’s papa), General Wil-
liams and the Prince of Wales had all
conspired in a course of conduct which proved
them to be rogues if they were not fools, and
mad if they were not drunk. And then the
jury would have found for the plaintiff against
each defendant, adding a rider to the effect that
Tummy was guilty of carrying the implements
of gambling in his trousers pocket,' but might
be released on the Queen’s recognisances until
an Act of Parliament was passed on his behalf,

Anti-strike prophecies were numerous that, as
soon as the Unionists were “ downed,” the
Employers’ Associations would take an un-
generous victor’s remorseless grip of the throat
of Labour and start reducing wages all round.
Brisbane Master Printers’ Association (some of
whom are employing excellent reporters at £2
per week) is the first squeezer to assert itself,
doing so in the belief that scores of unemployed
compositors are to be found in Brisbane, and
that the local Typographical Association has
neglected to sufficiently enlarge its membership
to withstand a “cutting-down” move. The
master printers have notified that, from and
after Ist July, they will reduce their pay-rates
to ;
Newspaper piecework, per thousand—Day-work, Is.;
night-work, 13d. Stab rates—7os. per week on morning
papers, and 555. per week on the evening papers and in
jobbing offices, with a proportionate reduction of rates
tor overtime.
This represents an all-round reduction of Id.
per thousand in piecework, and of ss. per week
wages. In the eyes of a corpulent syndicate,
£3 10s. a week seems a magnificent reward for
continuous all-night work involving the impair-
ment of health. If the Brisbane Typographical
Union attempts to combat this gratuitous,
greed-impelled attempt to reduce wages, the
Master Printers, if they are true to the tradi-
tions of the Employers’ Unions, will have the
unadulterated gall to rise up and declare that
the compositors started the strike, that they
had been for months organising it, and
that the masters are acting purely on the de-
fensive, for the glorious principle of “ freedom
of contract ” and for the hallowed right to con-
trol their own businesses.

The population of Ireland is still declining. The
present census shows about half-a-milliou less
inhabitants than did the last one, and the
total returns are now something over four
millions—just about half what they were half a
century ago ! The world in general is filling up
fast and people are swarming to Africa, .Aus-
tralia, and South America, but though Ireland
is more fertile, and more beautiful, and
probably more healthy than either of t he three,
nobody wants to go there, and everybody wants
to get away. The blessings of British rule
have made Ireland almost the only place on
earth that no human being will live in,
if he can find any other place to go to.
Even in Poland, Turkey, Spain, Mexico,
and similar countries which Englishman holds
up as models of hideous misgovernment, popu-
lation keeps increasing, which is a sufficient
proof that these lands are at least habitable,
but Ireland is practically uninhabitable. Not
even a billion tons of Tory drivel about the
happiness and prosperity of Ireland under
British coercion can weigh against this one
unanswerable fact of the ceaseless decrease of
the Irish population. No nation ever yet rose
up to make a unanimous exodus from a land of
liberty and happiness ; lands of that description
are scarce, and if Ireland was even one-tenth as
well-governed as the British Tory asserts, t here
would be a continuous influx of population from
other countries, instead of a continuous over-
flow.. The British Christian has probably ex-
tinguished as many conquered nations as any
other Christian on earth, and about fifty years
-more of tyranny, landlordism, snufflebusting,
and hypocrisy, will add one more to the list.
Them the last of the Irish people will have
vanished into some other continent, and be
absorbed by some other race, and the English
policeman will prowl through the great desert
of Connaught and find nobody to arrest, and
the English landlord will howl throughout
Leinster and find nobody to evict, and the
English magistrate will lose Ids job because
there will )>e nobody to hang, and a great claim
will pervade .the whole land of Erin.
The following is an interesting extract from a
book, recently published,in England :
The first tendency we .notice of the military profession
is that it makes a soldier more, or less a machine. “His
■not to reason why, his hut to do and die.” To be “ lord
of himself” is often, as Lord Byron found to Ids cost, a
“heritage of woe ” to a man, and it is not a bad tiling for
him to be wound up like a clock and made to go right;
hut this winding-up process converts him into, it may be,
:a noble machine, but a machine still. Nearly everything
which a soldier does he Is ordered to do. He is put down fol-
ia new pair of boots or tunic without being asked whether
he wants them or not. His dinner conies to him as though
it were manna from heaven. He is told when to get his hair
■cut, and when to put on or take off the several parts of
his outfit. At length lie ceases almost entirely to think
for himself, and puts childlike trust in the “Queen’s
Regulations.” Poor Tommy I I have seen him standing
for nearly an hour at a railway station, keeping all the
time a heavy valise on his back because no one told him
to take it off, and lie could not himself originate enough
thought to do so. On Christmas Day an old soldier was
•carrying a plum-pudding from the cook-house to his
barrack-room. A sergeant who happened to bo behind
him shouted out in joke, “ Attention 1 ” Down went the
hands of this creature of habit to his side, and down went
the dish.
No wonder the imported British loyalist pines
for the day when Australia will own a big
standing army of its own. For the true and
genuine soldier is impervious to unpleasant
doctrines about independence, human equality,
socialism, and the dignity of humanity—-just as
impervious as a threshing-machine or a boiler,
and from exactly the same cause—and his want
of thinking capacity keeps him going straight
ahead all the time.
In the British House of Commons, a few days
ag >, some rampant iconoclast brought forward
a motion for taxing the ground-rents of London,
and thus compelling the pompous British
millionaire to contribute a trifle to the revenue,
and, strange to say, the Tory party only mus-
tered a majority of 26 votes to throw out this
ni revolutionary project. Evidently, even the
i slow-going Parliament of England has some-
capacity for better things, and sooner or later
something is going to happen te the British
landlord. The ground-rents of London now-
amount to fifteen millions per annum, and they
are increasing with such amazing rapidity that
another generation will sec the annual total
raised to £30,000,000 or more. Most of this
represents steals pure and simple that is to
say, that neither the present owners nor their
ancestors paid a solitary cent for the property,
Some ancient king stole'it from the nation, and
gave it away to the principal shady female of
his harem, generally for no reason save thfit the
recipient ought to have been hanged, and the
king should have been hanged also! Neither
the shady female nor her descendants 'effected
any improvements on the land, and, therefore,
£15,000,000 per annum is merely the charge
which is levied on the people whose presence
has given value to the land, for the privilege of
making the said land valuable. The riot pus
and insurrectionary proposal, that a dead king
and a dead harlot are not sufficient reasons for
paying all this money, is no doubt a lamentable
surprise to the British landlord, and when the
Tory scrapes through by 26 votes only, things
are hot so promising as they used to be. But
the British duke can seldom sec anything that
is further removed than the point of his own
old-port-tinted nose, and probably it never
occurs to him that, in another generation, a
shouting mob may hang him, feet-uppermost,
on a clothes’-line, and keep him there till all
the millions of his unearned increment drop out
of him, and then shake him by the heels on the
edge of the pavement till they shake the title-
deeds off him. Yet something like this is
coming, even if the title-deeds have to be blasted
out of the landlord with dynamite.
The Duke of Westminster is refusing to renew
the expired leases of pubs, on his J/mdon pro-
perty. As this rich man practically throws
away the value of .the license each time he
wipes out a grog-shop, fie has some hollow pre-
tensions to assume virtuous frill op the subject,.
Yet, allowing that he is acting for what he con-
siders “ the good of his masses," it is obviously
scandalous that any private person should have
the power.to bar any authorised tradeover a
jlargc tract of densely-populated earth. West-
minster, in fact, is merely giving a respectable
illustration of the great landlord evil. He
has no more niqral right to abolish publicans
with a stroke of bis agent’s pen, than a vege-
tarian duke would have to decree, in the same
.way, the suppression of butchers. A future
"Westminster may easily become—unless some-
thing happens in the meantime —the owner: of
all London, and exclude doctors from his
estate, on the ground that they are, as Tolstoi
maintains, the root of all social'evil. Even as
matters stand now it is possible for a small
clique of titled London autocrats, 'of. a” few
dozen heads of county families, to starve out
the metropolis, or reduce the rustics to shadows,
by forbidding their tenants to keep any descrip-
tion of shop. Again, by a preconcerted arrange-
ment of leases the noble ring could shut up any
number of factories and generally play Sheol on
a given date, just as they can, and do occasion-
ally, close down coal mines here and there.
This ostensibly moral behaviour on the part of
Westminster will surely attract some more
nasty radical attention to the immorality of the
system which sanctions it. His legal right to
close a public-house is no less shamefully wrong
than is the right of that bloated “sport,” the
Duke of Hamilton, to prohibit, as he did pro-
hibit a while ago, a certain religious sect from
erecting their little tabernacle on the Isle of
Arran, or some such place, where he holds the
fee simple in everybody’s soul.
Professor Lawbie, of Hyderabad, declares
that deaths from chloroform are really due to
the improper rjiethod of administration. He
contends that Same’s method, which Lister
subsequently sanctioned by adopting, is almost
absolutely safe. Under this system tlie respira-
tion only is attended to, while in the ordinary
method the pulse as well as the respiration is
watched. Lawbie concludes “ that the appli-
cation of chloroform in ordinary cases is almost
criminal unless Same’s method is followed.”
He claims that chloroform has been given in
this way by him and his students many thou-
sands of times without a single fatality, and
refers in support of his position to the Commis-
sion appointed by an Indian Prince, which,
carrying out experiments on animals, demon-
strated that chloroform kills dogs by paralysing
the respiration, never by paralysing the heart.
Needless to say the result, conflicting as it did
with English views, was not accepted in
London, and another Commission was accord-
ingly appointed, to which the Lancet sent out
a member. The Lancet man held the conven-
tional view as to the action of chloroform upon
the heart; but the investigation occasioned a
reversal of his opinion. For this paragraph a
Melbourne Professor, holding University office,
is responsible, and we would just like to know
now, speaking as laymen what the doctors who
are associated with two or three late unfor-
tunate occurrences have to say on the point.

Cablegram: —“The Regent of Manipur and
the Seniputti or Commander-in-Chief are to be
tried on the Ist June. The prisoners will be
allowed the assistance of a British officer in con-
ducting their defence.” The prisoners’ country
was invaded by British officers who came to
haul down the Government and construct a new
one. They were shot at by British officers, and
killed some of these same officers in return, and
then they were arrested by more British officers,
and British officers have annexed their country,
and finally they are to be persecuted by British
officers and judged by British officers. Also
their defence will be conducted by British
officers; and as the only defence they can
make is that no British officer had any right
to be in their country at all, and.as no British
officer could advance such a defence without
getting iuto trouble for mutinous language and
high treason, they won’t really be defended at
all. And, finally, they will be shot by British
officers, and the inscription oh their tombstones
will set forth that they died of too much British
officer, and they will depart to run up against
red-hot British officers in Gehenna.


To the Australian Labour Party.
Fellow Malcontents : — The present social
order is bad. We are agreed upoxx that. It
must be changed. How are we going to change
it, and to what? We all have our plans of
x eform ; evex-y one of xxs carries ixx his pocket a
scheme that only wants “ be it enacted ” at the
head of it, and an authorisation signature at
the foot of it to regenerate the world. But the
li-ouble is to get the signature. Your signature
and mine, beloved brother, would ho no more
use there than, alas, they are at the bottom of
a cheque. If this country were a despotism,
and we were the benevolexxl despot, wc should
want no endox-sement. But seeing that it is a
democracy, our paper is xxo good unless wc can
get the people to back it. Doxx’t lose sight of
that fact. The country doesn’t believe ixx yoxxr
scheme or mine ixx its entix-ety. If the country
did, yoxx and I would no longer be advanced
Ihixxkei-s. We should think what everyone clso
thinks. The coxmtry will not accept our
schemes until we make it believe in them.
That is a long job and a big job. It is no uso
trying to force the new social ox-der down the
throats of the people in the lump. But there
is no reason why we shouldn’t do it piece-meal,
# # *
The business divides itself up into two depart-
ments. First we must make the people believe
that a thing ought to be done ; thoxx we must
make Parliament do it. First advocate, then
legislate. The order must not be reversed.
The work of Parliament commences where the
wox-k of education finishes. To talk in Parlia-
ment about unpopular reforms is a waste of
onex-gy and a waste of time. There is plenty of
work for the man who will set himself to carry
out popular reforms. Make for yourself a xnap
as clear as you caxx of the existing constitution
of society, then make another of that coxxstitu-
tion which yoxx desire to substitute and which
yoxx have in yoxxr mixxd as axx ideal. The differ-
ence between the one we have and the oixe yoxx
want is the grouxxd that you have to, cover by
reform. A good deal of it you will find has not
been accepted by pxxblic opinioxx—people don’t
believe ixx it. As to that your present business
is only to educate. You must begin by making
people believe iu it. But a great part is sure
to remain xxpou which you will find everybody
in, agreement with you-, or upon which, at any
x;ate, yoxxr Opponents will be few. That is the
sphere of legislation. It is a battle-ground oxx
which yon must succeed, if you only know how
to, manage your forces.
* *
It all lies ixx the management.. The mistake
w.e make is to mix up what we want the people
to, believe with what they do believe—the
measures w.e want them to approve of with the
measures we and they want Parliament to pass.
When the bixlk of the people are in favour of a
particular course, the man who advocates it ixx
Parliament, has an irresistible power behind
him,; but if he couples with it some private fad;
of Ins own, he simply fools away his chaxxce.
\yhei; Parkes 'wanted) to make sure of the
defeat of a xnqasure of electoral reform —the
abolition of, plural voting—that he had to
firing in,against fiis will, he incorporated in it
tjxe principle of woman-suffrage. Tfie whole
Bill was then.bound to go by the board, and the
crafty old man kxxew it,; not necessarily because
woman suffrage is a bad thing, but because
people are not yet educated np.'tp it. What
Parkes did oxxt of guile, the average social
reformer does every day out of mere softheaded-
ness, Not satisfied XYith taking the nuts out of-
the jar one by one, he tries fox- a fistfull and
ends by getting nothing.
When we have made up our minds \y hat we
want tp do, it remains to settle how it is to be
done, Hpw are those of you who get into
Parliament to, act? Whichever party comes
into power as the outcome of the elections—P
am ,speaking now with an eye particularly to
New South Wajes—there will be no attempt on
the part of the Government to deal with any
big social question. Neither Parkes nor
Dibbs will lift a finger, for example, to touch
the relations between labour and capital or to
diminish by a feather-weight the power of
privilege and vested interests, unless forced to
do so. The business of the real representatives
of the people is to force them. There is one.
way in which that can be done if you are united
enough and fairly resolute. It is in substance
the way that a numerically insignificant Irish
party has Dreed an English Parliament into an
attitude o( deferential, though occasionally
mutinous, civility. Here difficulties would not
be so great as those the Irish party had to face.
Against, them were arrayed the racial and
religious prejudices or the stupid indifference of
a nation ; you would have the bulkof the nation
at your back. '
# # #
Organise, then, and lay your plans. Agree
upon some definite and feasible instalment of
ref firm, and pledge yourselves to put it through.
You can do it if you will. Twenty of you could
make yourselves absolutely masters of the New
South Wales Assembly if yon banded yourselves
together' with one single object, and let nothing
under Heaven turn you from it. You would
hold the balance of power, and every Ministry
would be at your mercy. Let the Government
know plainly the price of your support. “ The
Bill—or your life.” To the Government that
will give you your Bill, and push it through,
sell yourselves unscrupulously, like a regiment
of Swiss or Hessians. If you would attain free-
dom by means of parliamentary Government,
you must conform to parliamentary usages. In
war the soldier merges his individuality, in the
regiment; in the parliamentary battle the
individual must sink himself in the party. If
your conscience won’t stand it, stop out. There
is another road to reform open to you—red
revolution. Let your conscience choose : there
is the bloodless parliamentary method on the
one hand—on the other, dynamite and barri-
cades.
# # #
Do you want reform, or do you not ? If you
do, you must take the means that are at your
hand. “ Do evil that good may come?” Yes,
my Christian friend, precisely, if the only
alternative is to do good that evil may continue.
Wholesale murder is a crime, even when it is
gilded with the name of war; but if it saves a
nation’s freedom, it needs no justification. The
protectionist who votes against his conscience
for freetrade may feel pretty mean over it* but
if he gets in return a Factories Act that will
save thousands of little children from being
robbed of their youth, he can hold up his head
without a blush in the presence of the strictest
Pharisee who gives voice for the perpetration
of infant slavery, and keeps his conscience
clean. “ Paris, said the Protestant Henry of
Navarro, “ is well worth a mass,”
# * #
With a practicable policy, a close organisa-
tion, a good general and plenty of determina-
tion, yon might do great things. You
mu*/ renounce office and the hope of office. Every
man of you should he pledr/ed to take no portfolio
either in the, existniy Ministry or the ensuing one.
In that respect your votes, whether to keep a
Government in or turn it out would be equally
disinterested, The loss you talk the bettor.
One spokesman should at almost any time be
enough for a body of men representing only one
idea, t The powerful party in Parliament is the
party that votes in a dumb phalanx. It would
be easy to enlarge upon this topic, but it is
quite unnecessary, None of these suggestions
arc new, Everybody who knows any-
thing of Parliamentary life knows they
embody the one way that has yet
been found effectual to overcome the dead-
weight of parliamentary indolence and dis-
inclination for reform. It is only by adopting
some such plan that members who are returned
on the strength of democratic professions can at
once give evidence of their sincerity and sub-
stantially advance the popular cause. Without
organisation the men who belong to the party
of progress are absolutely useless in the legis-
lature. They might as well save their breath,
or turn it to more profitable account hawking
newspapers. They are voices crying in the
wilderness. Now, where is the organiser?
Where is the Australian Parnell? —Yours,

The United States Congress has decreed that
in future no idiot or diseased person shall be
allowed to enter the republic either by land or
sea, nor shall any foreign criminal, “reformed”
or otherwise, any pauper or person who- seems,
likely to become a pauper, or anybody whose
passage has been paid for him, either in whole
or in part, be permitted to land on American
territory under any pretext whatever. And, in
addition to this, Congress is seriously consider-
ing the desirability of putting a heavy tax on
all immigrants—such a tax as shall practically
wipeout immigration for good and all. The,
great pauper-exporting business is about played
out, and it is time it were, for a meaner swindle
never existed. It was never anything more
than the old game of throwing your dead dog
over into the next man’s yard, and now the
next man is starting to throw the dog back
again. The English papers- are using very
strong expressions about this new American
legislation, but that was only to be expected
the individual who heaves the defunct animal
across the fence in the first place always pro-
fesses to think that the man next door is fond
of dead dogs, and hag been pining for the beast
all along, but when his neighbour whizzes the,
dog back !-!
Item, full of> hidden significance; from the:
Queensland papers :
At the Charleville police court to-day sjx unionists were
fined>£2s each or,five months’ imprisonment, for having a
quantity of fresh mutton in their possession for which
they were unable satisfactorily to account; also for having
a quantity of kerosene with which it was, suspected they
intended to commit a felony.
The Bulletin foretold several weeks ago that
Queensland unionism, would presently come to.
grief over this .fine old law, and the prophecy is.
coming true. No doubt the-magistrate who,
passed the sentence has also got kerosene in his
possession, and to a dead certainty .he is also,
possessed of property for which he can’t- satis-
factorily account* for nobody ever can bring
forward indubitable evidence that everything he,
owns was lawfully acquired; but then nobody,
will ever issue a warrant against the magistrate,
on that pretext. And' when some of the
unionists are. arrested for “ conspiracy,” and,
some more for refusing to “ move on,” and
another lot are run in for standing or sitting,
somewhere, which will be defined as “ unlawful
assemblage,” and a fresh batch are taken for
trespass, and a few hundreds are sent' to gaol
for having their own property in their possession,
the same being “reasonably,suspected,’’without,
a shadow of evidence, of being stolen, and a,
whole crowd are jugged for being somewhere,
with the supposed intention of committing a
felony, and finally the Riot Act is read to the ,
rest and they are trodden on and, crippled by
the troops before eah get .out of the road —
then there will,be very little of the great .strike,
left. For among the old brutal class laws which
no one has sense or energy enough to repeal,
there are statutes enough to authorise, tire, arrest
and imprisonment, of every working-man in
Australia whenever, any fool > of a magistrate,
sees fit to issue a warrant to that .effect,

suicided' up throat-cutting, This is
curious. The original Judas hanged himself.
Richmond Thatcher—“ Dick ” to his per-
sonal friends—old journalist, globe-rambler,
theatrical agent, publican, &c.-and brother of
the clever Thatcher of the old .digging-times, died ;
in Sydney on 9th inst., of Bright’s disease. A
reckless Bohemian of the most careless type, Dick
panned out in hard days. Comparatively deserted
by his friends, he kept a cynical stiff upper-lip in ‘
the most penurious circumstances. In his time,
Dick had been editor of Bathurst Independent,
Newcastle Herald, and had worked for the
Sydney Evening News, the Empire, Melbourne
Standard and a host of minor journals, His peu
used to drip cronk humour and cronker satire
resplendent with brilliant but false lights from a,
misanthropic and also disordered brain.. Latterly,
he lived almost entirely in unrea 1 i ties, a sojourn"
in a lunatic asylum having distorted the bent of
his always volatile genius. He had experienced
the extremes of current affluence and j absolute
destitution, and there is scarcely a nook in Aus-
tralia from Sweer’s Island, Carpentaria, to Yorke’s
Peninsula in the South where he was not well ,
known, As a raconteur jhe was sui gcperis.

At Sydney Garrick; the Oharringtons
abandoned “ The New Magdalelx ” on Wednesday
of last week for Pinex-o’s “The Money-Spinner,”
a tale of card-sharping, fair and innocent decoys,
seedy touts, true love and tommyrot; a play of
considerable change and much merit, not new and
yet not familiar to Sydney playgoers. Janet
Achux-ch, in the title rdle, was a pleasing example
of filial obedience overmastered by sweethearting
devotion—the sort of best girl who doesxx’t mind
outraging her xxatural instincts and bheatixxg at
cards to save her embezzling husband fx-oxn gaol.
Her acting was natural, vivacious and, in the
emotional parts, intense. Emma Bx-onton, as
Dorinda, the slangy sister of the Money-Spinner,
was the sort of jolly girl no fellow would
mixxd takixxg to a picnic, and her jealousy never
took a tragic hue. Meta Pelham, as Margot,
made much of a minor part. Charles Charringtoxi,
as Baron Croodle, was a boistei'ous impersonation
of that chattering old adventurer, but his mock-
seixtiment had xxot the slightest semblance of even
assumed geuuiness, while his rigidity of deliver -
axxce marred his best efforts, Herbert Elernming,
the honest noodle, Lord Kengussio, was natural,
even, and always well within himself, evincing
a highly commendable power of self-restraint.
H. R. Jewett, as a commonplace lover axxd
pardonable villain, was not the success that
he usually is when he poses as a cynical,

as the mysterious detective, “ the Ferret,” was

tout, did justice to his small part. “Adrienne

“ The County Fair” has been buzzing
along merrily at Her Majesty’s, and the homely
but quaint picture it gives of life on a New Eng-
land (U.S.A.) farm, draws laughing approval
nightly. Frank J. Currier’s Otis Tucker, the
Yankee farmer who bashfully spoons the mistress
of his husky heart for 14 years, is an individual-
istic creation worthy of a granite monument. His
facial obtuseness is indefinably transmitted, and
he never shakes off one fold of the personality
with which, he has clothed himself. Sadie String-
ham, the middle-aged maid of Otis Tucker’s heart,
fairly effervesces with the contradictory spurts of
a quick-tempered but kind-hearted Yankee farm-
mistress, and her nightgown evolutions in search
of the cause of a noise which she “saw ’’ comprise
some masterly gymnastics. Roland Watts-Phillips
speaks and acta- more like a city girl of refined-
education than one brought up on a farm. The
race-illusion, in which four horses are apparently
galloping their hardest and “never getting no
for’arder,” elicits nightly applause, which is con-
tinued’till Cold Molasses is declared the winning
horse and the liquid'atoi; of Miss Abby’s mortgage.

The enterprising management at the
Qyclorama have suddenly discovered, that the
long-legged grey nag, KiHarney, that has so long
advertised the show in a coat of, many colours,
actually took part in , the battle of Gettysburg as
the five-year old charger of General Hunt, under
the napie of “Grapes.” Gettysburg was-only
fought 28 years ago, and the matter is proved
beyond doubt by the animal’s resemblance, to a
quadruped on the canvas.

“ The Profligate,” which London critics
tried to establish amongst the immortals of,
dramatic literature, was produced at Melbourne
Bijou last week. Ibsen’s influence makes itself
severely felt in this overrated arrangement of false
creations. Pinero had probably, been, sitting up
for forty-eight hours, with the works of the
Scandinavian sage, before he lay down and
dreamed, , this elegant fable in four acts. It is
certainly not the effort of a wide-awake optimist,
but of a drowsy cynic, trying for the moment to
imagine things that never were nor will be, and
amusing himself by writing counter to the lessons
of human nature. Pinero lacks the Ibsenic faith
in absurdities, however. Although “The Profli-
gate ” is interesting in its action, and contains
many smart and beautiful lines, it has not the
genuine ring of the other gentleman’s plays.
Briefly, the story is that of a . cold, methodical
Hbertule mfcde fanatically good by marriage Willi
an abnormally innocent girl. A weak and
foolish combination of circumstances caused the
loving wife to pick up one of his former victims and
discover that the idol of her heait was a “betrayer ’
before he reformed. Then she rocks to and fro,
like a boat on troubled waters, and says “deny
it! ” with the persistency of a minute-gun. The
profligate is too repentant to deny it, she says
“go!”—and begets. There arc two endings to
the play, and both have been tried at the Bijou.
One night the profligate took poison; next night
ho jibbed at the prussic acid just previous to his
Wife finding him on his knees and forgiving him.
The former termination, being the most prepos-
terous, is the more consistent with the rest of the
story, but the vox populi. has declared in favour of
the latter. A yoUng lawyer friend and adorer of
the innocent heroine behaves like a lunatic when-
ever he gets an opportunity, and “ the girl who’s
been betrayed” is a hopeless case. She Wanders into
the lawyer's office, asking him to find her seducer,
whose real name she doesn’t know, but whose face
she sketches on the blotting pad. Charles Cart-
wright’s face is not an easy one to delineate with
ten strokes of a pencil, yet the agitated attorney,
trembling as he glances over the girl’s shoulder,
recognises the likeness and says—“ O, my God 1 ”
What does he next? Does he say—“ Young lady,
a brilliant future awaits you on The Bulletin ;
take an engagement at £2O a week and sin no
more?” No. He lets her walk out of the office
and become a sort of superior domestic to an
offensive, but cute and high-toned stout female,
in whose service she faints by the road-side.
So it comes about that the heroine takes com-
passion on this “ ruined ” spinster, and when
her story becomes known the heroine’s brother
developes a consuming passion for his profligate
relative’s cast-off mash. Perhaps he marries hex-
after the curtain falls, in which case they must
all form an interesting family-circle between
them. As “The Profligate” is now withdrawn,
the clever performance of the principals can be
hastily dismissed. Miss Nether-sole's innocence
is associated with her usual pretty affectations of
manner; Cartwright plays his unreal character
with hard force, and Titheradge does his best for
the lawyer. Miss Emma Temple is a pleasing
failux-e as the betrayed damsel, and Boucicault,
with Miss Watt-Tanner, throw natural side-lights
on the second act. Four nights of “Moths” are
to close on Friday. On Saturday they play “ The
Idler ” for a wind-up of the season.

A great financial success for “ Cleopatra ”
was assured before the olive-skinned queen
stepped out of her barge at Melbourne Princess’,
yet the management may have made a mistake in
producing Sardou’s tragedy so soon. Hundreds
of people were waiting to see Bernhardt m what
they imagined to be her finest character, and a
largo proportion of those hundreds are now at a
loss to understand the furore she created at the
outset. Worse still, they go around town mocking
at the boom; and sighing for the shillings they
paid to see a spectacle that certainly didn’t dazzle
the general eye (though its artistic merits are
many) and a “star ” who gave them but a faint
notion of her brilliancy. Expecting to be stag-
gered they paid the extra prices for a show in
which the all-round efficiency of the company,
bax-ring only the dandy Marc Antony, was almost
as noticeable as Sara’s individual perform-
ance, but the muddle-headed multitute pay little
heed to small parts spoken in a foreign tongue.
They kept one eye on Sara and the other on the
scenery, and—as already stated —neither optic
was dazzled. Should business wane during the
current week the misfortune can be fairly attri-
buted to “ Cleopatra,” or rather to the vain hopes
excited by irresponsible rumours. With three
fresh plays on the programme and “Camille”
again for Saturday night, intelligent interest in
Sara will remain at fever heat anyway.
Promises as yet unfulfilled are “Adrienne
Lecouvreur” to-night (18th), “Frou-Frou” to-
morrow (19th), and “ Joan of Arc”’for the Satur-
day matinee. It is after being burnt soft in the
market-place of Rouen that Bernhardt rises from
her ashes to die with her wonderful backthrow
as La Dame aux Camelias. On Monday they
produce “Theodora” on a scale of magnificence
which can be confidently looked forward to, inas-
much as Brunton and Goateher have painted the
scenery on- the premises. Theodora is a great
character—gruesome, but tremendous. So another
rush for seats will bring a memorable season to a
qlose, and the actress, who is divine even in the
teeth of Evangelist Varley seems certain to go out
as large a lioness as she came in. By the way,
“ Cleopatra,” has apparently cured Sara of hex-
disposition to irritate the public between acts.
Her proud spirit rose against the thought of ladies
fussing out whilst she, Sarah, was on the stage,,
and she allowed the performance to terminate by
H.IQ after that noisy Monday mentioned in our
last..

and Spagnoletti says he saw her sailing down the Block
with a string of French actresses like a young ladies’

Pinero’s “Profligate,” Melbourne Bijou, again raises
the question of publishers and managers interfering with
the denouement of novels and plays. Cartwright acts the
piece with the unhappy ending, as Pinero wrote, and not
the happy one insisted on by Manager Hare. Beerbohm
Tree spoilt the “Dancing Girl” the same way, and the
London Adelphi audience made Boucicault spoil “The
Octoroon,” which powerfully ended with her death origi-
nally.
It was a great treat to hear Pinero read his “Profligate,’’
in London, a few' months ago. He is a beautiful reader,
though a very indifferent actor, which is often the case.
The two things hardly ever go together, and Irving has
killed elocution on the stage.
A quarter of a century ago Irving was rather noted in
Bill Sikes, acting it to Toole’s “Artful Dodger,” and
Frank Thornton told the writer this was the first thing
that drew his attention to Irving.
What occasioned the recent interminable act-waits at the
Melbourne Princess’ ? Sara’s make-up is very simple,
after the first plastering. Anyone would think each dress
was fixed on with a thousand pins.
Memory of Irving’s first night in “ The Bells” We
strolled into the Lyceum, not expecting much, save from
the afterpiece of “Pickwick,” in which Irving was so
funny as Jingle. Gradually this Mathias seized upon the
house—fascinated, calvanised, like Kean’s first night in
Shylock. In the third act the performer’s fortune was
made. Memorable was it to see him stagger out, in his
shirt-sleeves, and receive the thunderous congratulations.
And how' miraculous was the transition to his Jingle ! In
a few nights he yielded it to Chas. Warner.”
Alice Sydney Burvett has been trickling over the piano
at Melbourne Town Hall. A blonde young lady of distin-
guished appearance, familiar on the Collins-street Block
With henna and her little poodle, which she leads by a
silvery chain. Miss Burvett advertises, indignantly, her
contempt for the Melbourne critics, and indeed the ring
against her is inexplicable. Alice, an Australian, has had ■
hard fights, and somehow the Melbourne writers have sat
on her spare frame, and tried to crush her out in the past,
but she won’t be squelched and is resolved to score. She
duetted the “ Marche Hongroise ” with Kowalski himself
at Paris Chatelet Theatre.

Thus does London Telegraph tramp on Ibsen. There
is a vigour about it which is not common in the
Telegraph: “Outside a silly clique there is not the
slightest interest in the Scandinavian humbug. . . The
decent householder puts his garbage and offal outside the
door. But some w'ell-bred and educated dog is sure to
foot OVet the pile, and to bury his nose in the nastiest
morsel. The better bred and educated the dog the more
he relishes the worst scrap of carrion. Cannot we leave
this muck-heap to the educated and much-ferreting dogs?
But what would have been the good to hunt away these
educated individuals from the Ibsen dust-bin ? They
know it is nasty, and they pretend to conceal their love of
nastiness with a love of literature.” The Telegraph isn’t
much good at illustration, anyhow. The assertion that a
“decent householder ” habitually stacks offal in front of
his door to be rushed by lost dogs seem to suggest that
the paper grew up in Petticoat-lane.
Why can’t Bernhardt’s plays be cut down ? Look at
“CRopatre,” set to play nigh four hours! “Cut the
dialeck and get to the ’osses,”as Ducrow said. “Fedora”
is a splendid model of the scud under bare poles.
Baccarat is the card game played in “Camille” at
Melbourne Princess’.
Grand operas which have never been heard in Australia:
Wagner s “ Tannhauser,” “ Tristan und Isolde,” “ Walkyre,”
“Siegfried,” “Parsifal,” “ Rienzi,” “ Meistersinger ; ”
Verdi’s “Don Carlos,” “ Otello ; ’ Sullivan's “Ivanhoe;”
Gounod’s “Romeo and Juliet;’ Gluck’s “Orfeo ’ —very
successful in London this season.
Bernhardt’s scenery is rather faded, but she relishes her
familiar setting, and so the mounting of “LaTosca”is
quite shabby beside that in the Potter season. When she
gets in front of Goatcher and Hennings, for “ Theodora,”
she will be ready to abandon her old stuff.
Williamson and Garner are munching over Sullivan's
“Ivanhoe,’'more about which will be heard, when Bern-
hardt, Nelly Farren and Fred. Leslie are moved on. Next
years big thing will be “Ivanhoe," with, maybe, the
“Cigale” for this Christmas. “Ivanhoe” will require a
double cast—but, indeed, there must be two or three
fresh voices got to sing it at all, and a prima donna is
indispensable.
Visiting theatricals don’t half like the Melbourne
Princess’ stage-door, on the front street, Spring. This
was a device to abolish masherdom, but seems to reduce
theatricals too much to common dust, with the gloze and
glamour gone.
Commentary on the current ignorance of Shakespeare.
Lamonby, having lent to a friend his volume of Shake-
speare containing “Taming of the Shrew,” trusts to a
Yankee quotation of some length from that play, and
sends it to the Argus as an illustration of Shakespeare
prigging from Marlowe, but the quotation doesn’t appear
at all in the play—nevertheless the Argus inserts it!
Whereupon James Smith, of course, comes down like a
thousand of bricks.
Our esteemed friend, H, H. Vincent, late manager for
Williamson and Garner, writes from London that he is
cosily settled there, on his return. He quickly dropped
into an engagement with Wyndham at the Criterion,
where he made a success right off with his Max
Harkaway, in the “London Assurance” revival. At the
latest, Vincent was acting Sir Oliver Surface in “The
School for Scandal.”

A London writer wants to know why unsuccessful plays
are so wildly and lengthily advertised with cooked press
notices and column “ ads.,” while successful plays are
advertised in an inch of space all round. He asserts that
this is the reason why, when a success has exhausted a
theatre’s clientele, business drops so suddenly.

“Ganz” violin willed to her by the great musician (won
at the Conservatoire, 1834) at a Sydney Y.M.C.A. concert
the other evening.
“Jack ” Beer Johnstone, the oldest actor on the British
stage, author of over 150 dramas and pantomimes, and
the father of Eliza Johnstone, died at London recently,
aged 89.

She intends to produce “Hun Wild,” probably at the

Lightning-calculator Moulton was pulled off his platform
at Balmain the other evening by some roughs. One leg
was broken in two places.
The operetta “Prince and Pedlar” was played by the
pupils of Fort-street Public School to a tremendous
audience at Sydney Her Majesty’s on Saturday last. The
pupils, trained by Mr. Turner, the head-master, played
and sang with the easy coolness of old hands, much to the
satisfaction of their relatives and friends.
The English census reveals the fact that Genevieve
Ward’s mother is still alive. She must indeed be very old.
Nothing more has been heard of the reported split at
London Lyceum. Like Royal Palaces, that home of
Thespis is perhaps not allowed to shelter a scandal, and
people are sworn to secrecy.
Thanks to Marion Lea and Elizabeth Robins, both
charming actresses, Ibsen’s “ Hedda Qabler”—pronounced
“ Gaybler ” —has had so large a measure of success at the
Vaudeville Matinees, that Tom Thorne is going to put the
play in the evening bill, “Money,” the time-worn, having
failed to attract. It is doubtful if “Hedda Gabler”will
draw an evening crowd, for truth to tell, the bulk of the
enthusiasts are professional “ dead-heads”—writes a
Bulletin contributor from London.
Ireland seems to rival America in the production of
good singers, we have Leslie Grotty, Barton McGuckin,
Esmond, O'Mara and others, and now D’Oyley Carte, says
our London correspondent, has unearthed another bari-
tone in the person of Mr. Arthur Seaton, who bids fair to
make a name for himself. He studied at the Regio
Conservatoire at Milan, where his teacher formed a very
high opinion of his voice and dramatic aptitude. Apropos,
a great number of young Irishmen, sons of men who
have “come down in the world,” have adopted the
profession of entertainers with considerable success.
Haddon Chambers is scooping the dollars. “ The Idler”
plays in London to about £2OO nightly, and his arrange-
ment is a very liberal one.
Yarley scarifies Bernhardt. An absurd mass of misap-
prehepsion, which five minutes’ chat with her would
dissipate.
Sara Bernhardt’s acting is a marvel in its restraint and
chastity. One sees plainly how English actresses merely
make most pieces an excuse to spread themselves out in
sensuous excess. There is not the faintest coarseness
in Bernhardt’s renditions, as in those of a Potter or a
Langtry.
Madame Simonsen wires from Milan that she has
obtained a first-class opera-troupe for Australia. If only
as good as the crowd which included Mesdames Ciuti,
Rebottaro, Tagliavia, MM. Lazzarini, Santinelli, D’Alba,
&c., it will pay, but really we want an Abbey to play the
Augustus Harris, or even the Lyster, to furnish Mel-
bourne and Sydney with a properly established grand
opera. All behind the times.
Albert Richardson, baritone, is back in Melbourne,
after many years. When grand opera was in vogue he
made a rise by bringing out his pupils in most ambitious
operatic programmes, as Grisis, Marios, and Lablaches in
embryo. Among them were Miss Nordt, Messrs. C.
Moule, Hannah, &o.

“ Cleopatra ” is a distinctly inferior play, not a scrap of
poetry—nothing but carpentry in authorship—and yet it
is hideously drawn out in political speeches. How is it
men will only be enchanted with the bad-’uns—“ Helen
of Troy” (The World’s Desire), “Cleopatra,’ and “Mary
Stuart ? ” Here are the three most historical women.
When Sara acted “Theodora” in Paris, it was said the
public indeed had a surfeit of them, for Masse’s “Cleo-
Eatia” was given at the Opera House, and the spectacular
allet of “Messalina” at the Eden. Faustine is the
newest Parisian play, exalting such creatures, in a Roman
Empress. “Catherine of Russia” was as bad as “Cleo-
patra ’’—but no scandal about Queen Elizabeth.
The Duke of Edinburgh recently played at a concert at
Bristol in aid of the District Nurses’ Society. Nurses are
people to whom the members of the Royal Family owe a
great deal. Alfred led the orchestra and played the
obligato to Braga’s Serenade ; but as R. S. Smytbe, who
was at the concert, did not offer him an engagement, the
distinguished amateur probably did not quite come up to
Camilla Urso. The Much Travelled went to Bristol to
hear Amy Sherwin sing Marguerite in “ Faust ” with
the Carl Rosa Co.
