We are so much accustomed to hear the praises of old-
time actors sung to the disparagement of modern players
that the following epigrammatic sketch of by-gone
worthies will be read with amusement“ Charles
Kemble was a silver-toned, sententious mannerist;
Edmund Kean was a stuttering, spasmodic mannerist ;
Macready and Phelps always grim and growling over their
bones; Charles Kean had a chronic cold in the head ;
Keeley was sleek and sleepy; Buckstone, a chuckler ;
Compton, funny as a funeral; Ben Webster, always im-
perfect, with a Somersetshire dialect; Charles Mathews,
a Mephisto in kid gloves and patent-leather boots ; and
Ryder, a roarer.”
Ada F itzroy, Melbourne Waxworks, is amazingly clever.
Her feats of memory are unique, and she does the Daven-
port Bros, feats to perfection.
Somebody wants particulars about Henry Neil Warner.
He must be nigh seventy now, teaching elocution, in the
States. When the Melbourne Royal opened, 1855, he was
a powerful opponent to G. V. Brooke, in parts like the
Corsican Brothers, but he became too much a Brooke
imitator.
Luckless Joubert had the Melbourne Alexandra designed
on the model of the finest Vienna theatre. Hence the
public favour, because everyone can see, whereas the
English idea seems to be that the cheap people mustn’t if
it can be helped. Observe the form of the theatre, like a
T, with the auditory broadside on to the stage, instead of
lengthwise, as usual.
Two Melbourne morning critics find Duquesne’s Scarpia
much inferior to Bellew’s, the third emphatically declares
the reverse, but fat Duquesne, an oily Tartuffe, is all
behind the point, sarcasm, and passion of Bellew in the
part. Duquesne is rather a stuffy substitute for Gamier,
Sara’s Parisian leading man.

rises every evening.

The ballet-master of the Viennese ballet “Rouge at
Noir,” having weeded his troupe of the older dancers
calmly replied to complaints: “ Ladies, this ballet is
‘ Rouge et Noir,’ not ‘ Trente et Quarante.”’
The latest development in the show business is keeping
awake for money. In Detroit, lately, at the Wonderland
Museum, six men started to abstain from sleep (a closed
eye indicates slumber) during seven days and nights for
£lOO purse. When the mail left the contest had lasted
five days and only two wide-awakes were left in, one of
these had gone slightly silly, but had been cute enough to
enter into a keep-one-another-from-sleeping union with his
antagonist.
Stanley’s Zanzibar boy, Sali, didn’t get on well in
America. He went into an Atlanta hotel and sat down,
and a man came and shook him. Says he: “1 said
‘What’s the matter?’ He said, ‘Get out, you ——
nigger.’ In England the Queen shook my hand and I went
to the Prince of Wales’ house. In this diotel they say,
‘ Get out, you nigger ! ’ ”
The law which excludes leg-shows, ballet and skirt*
dances, female circus-riders, &0., from Minnesota, also, of
necessity, prohibits “ As You Like It,” “Twelfth Night,”
and all the plays and operas wherein the heroine dons
man’s attire, or wherein the music of a man part is scored
for a woman’s voice.
Woe betide a woman whom stage fascination prompts
to barter away honour and home! Thirteen years
ago the world was shocked when the Earl of Desart
obtained a divorce against his wife because of her rela-
tions with Charles Sugden, the actor. Last mail brings
news of the ex-Countess’s second divorce. Her actor-
husband, for whom she lost all, deserted her and cohabited
with a servant.
“Poor Jonathan” makes little headway at
Melbourne Opera House, although that apotheosised
cook is full of tune and not altogether destitute of points.
But being something of an outrage on comic opera
tradition in the matter of costumes and business,
“Jonathan” must surely die. A censorious world con-
demns him without a trial, and the onlookers, metaphori-
cally speaking, turn their thumbs down at the Yankee
stranger when Vandergold threatens to destroy him in
the second act. And this notwithstanding that the
latter’s struggle to remember the fatal melody is a
very humorous conception. Snazelle, the ladies, and, in
a lesser degree, Leumane, have worked up their parts;
also the orchestra, under Hamilton Clarke, is a strong
feature in a dispiriting night’s work. Yet these things
are of small practical account in a half-filled house. One
feels sorry for “ Poor Jonathan,” as for a harmless in-
dividual gone wrong, or a tale that might have been
better told. “The Gipsy Belle” is in rehearsal and will
probably be wanted before another week runs out.
# * #
At the Alexandra they will take the muzzle off
“ The Trapper ” on Saturday night, when a grand all-
round slaughter of wild tribes, villains, and things is cer-
tain to ensue. “The Scout,” now at its last splash, is a
calm domestic drama as compared with “The Trapper,”
in which the historical tank swallows up creation at one
fell gulp, and the entertainment has to start anew with a
fresh catalogue of horrors. Frank Carver, his Injins and
other appurtenances, naturally improve as they grow
accustomed to the stage. Lily Dampier, too, can send a
bullet through the eye of a needle, and all the materials for
a regular booster are in readiness. Nellie Farren and
Fred. Leslie shuddered at the tank effects, the other
night, whilst yesterday’s (Wednesday) matinee gave Sara
Bernhardt an opportunity to understand that realism is
still in its infancy at the Porte St. Martin.

“ Betsy ” is due to re-appear at the Bijou next
Monday (22nd), with Brough, Anson, Bouoicault, and
Pattie Brown in the bill.
* * *
The Halles, having engaged the Victorian
Orchestra and laid themselves out to attract the mass of
music-lovers, had the Town Hall nicely packed on Satur-
day. Charles Hall 6 is, above all things, a great con-
ductor, and when he waves a baton around his. wife’s
violin solos in a bit of Beethoven’s best—well, the audi-
ence get as near to Heaven as most of them can expect to
reach. It is worth noticing that the management offers a
concession in price to Vic. Orchestra subscribers, who,
being the avowed supporters of classical melody, are able
to buy reserved stalls for two “ bob ” less than the quoted
price. This is a practical arrangement, with a moral
hanging thereto,
ft ft ft
Jude’s Musical Evenings at the Athenaeum turn
out to be chiefly remarkable for his skilful treatment of
the harmonium. Jude can do everything in reason, and
much that seems impossible, with this widely-neglected
instrument. Sounds of the rolling drum, bagpipes, and
silvery lute are conjured up from its cranky interior, and
when the performer finishes a fantasia there isn’t a mad
rush for the doors in anticipation of the offertory basin.
As a rule the harmonium is so dreadfully suggestive of a
collection. Jude has the hall for five more nights this
week, whilst the Bellringers chip in on Saturdays, as
already mentioned.
ft # *
Elsewhere the ball keeps rolling. The Victoria
Hall has taken to calling itself the “Berth of Mirth.”
Frank Clark and Slade Murray seem to be fetching them
into the Gaiety, and Miss Ada Fitzroy is a first-class
favourite at the Waxworks. She gives a pretty little
show in an especially pretty little way.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Albert Victor, the second Coming K., rode in a
military steeplechase recently, and finished seventh. Lots
of the other fellahs came fearful croppers, by the way,
some of them being grassed through their nervous
anxiety to leave a clear course for “ Collars and Cuffs,”
who is said to have sat out the affair, on a splendid
jumper, without betraying a gleam of interest.
* * #
If a colt and a filly were to make a dead-heat
for the A.J.C. or V.R.C. Derby, and didn’t run it off, they
would both have to carry 7st. 61b. in the Melbourne Cup,
provided they were entered and handicapped below that
weight. Consequently the filly would then have exactly
51bs. the worst of it with the colt, on their Derby running.
Year after year Tub Bulletin has called attention to this
tiny weak spot in the Melbourne Cup conditions. Sooner
or later the dead-heat we have suggested will come to
pass, and then the aggrieved owner will get on the spot,
and everybody will see, when the fact is right under their
noses, what this long-sighted journal perceives in the dim
distance. Five pounds too much in the saddle is a serious
matter when there is £lO,OOO at stake.
* * #
Should Ramage’s leg (what a bad pair he had to
be sure) not be well by the Spring meeting, who will steer
Carbine in his engagements? Hales is spoken of, but,
good horseman as he is, the public don’t like him in a big
field now'. In Derbys and Legers with a field of about
eight, Tommy is a perfect general—but in a big blocking-
race the cool, level-headed Ramage is the man.

The beneficiary was unable to be present at the
De Mestre complimentary race-meeting over Randwick
on Monday last. Owing to election excitement the
attendance was not large, about 4000 being present. Not
one favourite got home first, and several of the winners
were practically unbacked. J. R. Richardson’s Repeater(7st
51b) w'Ou “ The Tim Whiffler ” Handicap by a head from
Kelso's Denman. Lamond s gelding Johnnie won the
Navigator Handicap, a race for 2-year-olds, with 6st 111 b
on his back, by a neck from Fielder’s colt, Welcome. The
Selling Race was won by Payten s Reversion, the nearest
approach to a favourite among the winners of the day.
The Bookmakers’ Purse was pocketed by T. Ivory, by aid
of Sweetbit, a 5-year-old, with 6st 111 b, followed home at
a neck s interval by Dowling’s Helene, 3yre, 6st 111 b, and
Hop Bitters, 6yrs, 7st 71b. Herbert's Bex, syrs, Bst 71b,
won the De Mestre Handicap from Cornwell s Missie,
3yrs, 9st 31b, and Thompson s Labour, 4yrs, 9st 31b.

* # *
It is pathetic to notice how the Melbourne
sporting papers invariably describe a little lightweight
jockey as Master Whitty, because his father happens to
be a rather amateur kind of trainer. The English rags
are not so bad as this. Some of the “Home” trainers
have two or three liveried flunkeys to wait at table, but
if their youngsters get into the saddle for a race they are
listed under their surnames, pure and simple.
• * #
W. O. Hodgkinson advises the Toowoomba
Presbytery to attend race-meetings, as St. Paul did at
Tarsus, and thus personally superintend a crusade against
gambling:—
It doesn’t help the “boxing” interest to see
such things as this in the paper (Argus):— “ Choynski
drove Dooley on to the ropes, and then forced him round
to his own corner, where he used his right with telling
effect, and Dooley went down from a blow on the jaw. He
declined to take his full time, and came to his feet after
six seconds, when Choynski hit him on the neck, and he
fell again. He appeared quite dazed, but struggled to his
feet after four seconds, and Choynski, who had been hold-
ing his right far back, never gave him a chance, but
dashed it in with tremendous force on the point of the
jaw. Dooley fell in a sitting posture for a second or two,
and then dropped flat on his back, his head striking the
boards with a loud crash. He was unable to move, and
remained insensible after the blow for four minutes.”
Again (in another paper):—“For a considerable time
after being removed to his dressing-room Dooley was in a
dazed state. He was assisted to dress, but every now and
then would sit down and bury his head in his hands and
remain so for some minutes. It was half an hour after
the fight before he could speak sensibly.” This all reads
like murder in the first degree.

* # #
“ Pendragon’s ” first journalistic opening of any
note was given him, five-and-twenty years ago, on the
Glowworm, a London evening paper owned by the pro-
prietors of the Weekly Dispatch and the long-defunct
Illustrated Sporting and Theatrical News. For this
rag he penned' the notices of such plays as were scorned
by the regular critic, wrote racing-tips in prose and
verse, reported prize-fights, boat, foot and swimming
races, did general newspaper-touting, hashed up leaders
on emergencies, and occasionally assisted the proof-
reader. When the Glowworm died be had a rough time
till he became sporting-writer for the Dispatch.

When Gov. Kintore was at Port Darwin, the
secretary of the Katherine River Turf Club epistled him
for a subscription. Kintore never answered the note.
Before the vice-regal party left, Dr. Stirling donated 30s.
to the club, which thereupon added to its “kreckt kard’‘
the following:—
Notice Extraordinary.
Katherine Donkey Race, value 80s., donated jointly by
Lord Kintore and Dr. Stirling. Last horse, £1; first
horse, 10s. Distance, 1 mile ; catch weights. Com-
peting horses must not be valued over £5 by the
stewards. To be run the last day of the meeting, 25th
May, 1891. Post entry, 10s.
Could insult go further?
India, which already owed £206,000,000,
has just offered in London a 3 per cent, loan of
£2,600,000 at 92, and got it subscribed twice over
at £94 10s. One day England will lose India, also
the principal and interest of India’s loans. Mean-
while, however, India is ever so much more
respectable than Australia.
A startling rumour is cabled concerning
William O’Brien, M.P. for North-east Cork, at
present in prison for a breach of the Crimes Act
in Ireland. Mr. O’Brien has been twice married.
His first wife disappeared, it is stated, some
years ago, and believing that she was dead, he
married again last year. It is now stated that on
emerging from prison he will be “confronted
with ” his first wife.

If N.S.W. Treasurer M‘Millan has been
such a brilliant financier, where is the money?
The Treasury is empty; M’Millan has secretly
borrowed a million and a quarter; and New
South Wales’s next loan is already hypothecated
to the London Hebrews.

THE REASON WAS OBVIOUS.
WARM ADMIRER: “ Why are you so cold?”
There are signs without number that an
insidious and powerful effort is being made to
introduce the principle of land-grant railways into
democratic South Australia. There is not much
hope that the Playford Government will veto this
pernicious system, as they have already fixed up
a preliminary agreement with an expectant
syndicate. It is also not unlikely that Parliament
will approve of the transcontinental trunk-line
being completed on the land-grant principle.
Parkes was the real author of the proposal
to substitute “Prime Minister” for “Premier,”
and has spoken of himself on every possible occa-
sion as “Prime Minister of the great colony of
New South Wales,” making a large mouthful of
the pompous phrase. Parkes now repudiates
“Prime Minister.” Why ? Simply because (a
typical specimen of his miserable vanity) the
Premier of South Australia officially adopted the
thing first.
Premier Griffith has got the Queensland
Cabinet to consent to ask the Parliament of the
Northern colony to vote Queensland’s share of the
Australian naval tribute fund, hitherto, refused,
But the Parliament is likely not to “see it.”
Treasurer MTiwraith has acquiesced, and so
turned a complete political somersault on the
“ national ” policy with which he carried the con-
stituencies in ’BB, probably because the loan fiasco
has put him in a blue funk.
N. Z. Government is letting railway-con-
tracts to unemployed workers on the co operative
principle, at fixed rates. The first.of these con-
tracts left the men a balance of £l2OO for a-years
work, after paying 10s. a day to each man and 1-s.
a day to each foreman, although 27 more men were
employed than previous contractors had engaged
for similar work. Another illustration of how the
middlemen—absent in this instance —gobble up
the profits of labor. The new system may be pro-
fitably transplanted to Australia.
Queensland Government has had to crawl
before the London financial magnates and has
accepted % millions of 3i per cents (formerly
offered at 94) on terms amounting to something
less than 90 ! The Treasurer has got to pay up,
by June 30, £1,170,950, borrowed on debentures in
1886 by Griffith at 0 per cent. Two and a half
millions at 90 only means 2\ millions cash ; so'
that the present fatal loan will give MTiwraith
something just over a million down—all of which
is probably hypothecated in advance.

“ Now for the last 20 years, we have never seen
the Queen’s birthday pass without Imperial
honours being conferred on prominent politicians.
But on the last Queen’s Birthday, this ancient and

because it was known perfectly well that if these
distinctions were showered on the delegates [to the
Federal Convention], the people would have risen
with one voice and said that at the back of this
Federation business was the Imperial Govern-
ment.”

New South Wales is a glorious freetrade
country, and Parkes is her prophet. Salt, every
man’s necessity, is taxed 20s. a ton ; silks, the
fineries of the wealthy few, are free of duty. Tea,
the common people’s beverage, is taxed 3d. per
lb.; gold and silver plate, the splendours of the
pot-bellied monopolist, is free. Sugar is taxed,
according to variety, from £5 to £6 13s. 4d. pet
ton; carpets, for the gouty feet of privilege, are
free. Kerosene oil, the light of the poor, bears a
tax of Gd. a gallon; pianos and carriages, the
luxuries of the brow-beating classes, are free.
Bacon and cheese pay 2d. a lb. ; jewellery comes
in free. And so on for ever so many items.
Landed estates are untaxed, and the tariff of
N.S.W. freetraders is “ free ” only for the classes;
hot many chances are missed of so selecting the
items to be taxed that the workers pay 90 per-
cent. of the collections. Good old Free-tection 1
So the N.S.W, .Premier has been sending
a “touching message ” to the Canadian Dominion
Government in re the death of “ Old To morrow ”
Macdonald. We are not privileged up to date-to
inspect the message aforesaid, which was probably
a sickening specimen of Imperialistic slush and
utterly unfit for publication in N.S. Wales on the
eve of a general election ; but we have before us
the reply, which expresses gratitude for the
N.S.W. “touching etc.” One thing is certain:
an expensive cablegram quite unnecessarily
“touches ” the N.S.W. taxpayers. “ Sympathy”
—especially sham sympathy—could just as well
have been expressed per medium of a sixpenny
stamp, an envelope, a piece of paper, and a high
decimal’s worth of ink, as of an expensive cable- ’
gram paid for with loan-money and sent purely as
an advertisement for the old posturer who, now
that Macdonald has cleared out, stands alone as a
political humbug. However, “ the Hempire” ha*
to be considered.
In the very first speech uf N.S.W. the
electoral campaign Parkes said the one thing that
everybody knew to a certainty Parkes would say.
“ The men who voted against me,” he declared,
“ must be taken to have voted against Local
Government, against the Federation of Australia,
against the abolition of plural voting, and against
the cause of Labour.” The Bulletin has put in
some “h’s” and some lost grammar, but other-
wise that was exactly what the old man asserted.
And what he forgot to say was that his opponents
had declared against clothes because Parkes wore
clothes, and that when they voted the no-
confidence motion they voted that they would
never again have any hair, because the great Hi-
Ham is hairy; and that when they opposed Sir
Henry they also opposed the habit of washing
because Sir Henry washes himself—at least it may
be assumed that he does. But, then, as the old,
grey poet is not the only man on earth who washes
himself this argument falls through; and all the
rest of the arguments break down for just the
same reason.
G. H. Reid says he has looked through
the N.S.W. Statute-book to find an original idea
placed there by Parkes. Re has failed. Of the four
good measures in Parkes’name, the N.S.W, Public
Schools Act was taken from other acts; so with the
Municipalities Act, which was spoiled in being
plagiarised. The Public Works Act was the idea
of W. T. Poole, and the Railway Commissioners’
Act was taken from Victoria. Parkes, however,
has conceived three original ideas. The first was
the proposed Dead-House; the second was to call
N.S.W. “ Australia,” and the most recent one
was that worthy Australians should have peerages
as well as worthy men in England. In 1884, in
the Nineteenth Centuky, Parkes advocated
Imperial Federation, and suggested that the
names of the colonies should be dropped and that
they should be called 1 ‘ The British States of
Australia also that they should be welded
together by peerages. A true and damning in-
dictment.
Thus Gov. Hopefcoun, to Melbourne
Chamber of Commerce
It is not necessary for us to consider what would happen
if a great (British) naval disaster were to occur. Any-
thing short of absolute perfect success at sea, anything
short of our (the British) flag being triumphant, must
ruin all our trade, starve the people at home, and ulti-
mately break up the British Empire.
The mantle of Balaam in the matter of enforced
but reluctant prophecy hath descended upon
Hopetoun.
A memo, accompanying the letter notify-
ing the date of the annual dinner of the Victorian
Employers’ Union is marked “private,” and runs
thus;—
Dear Sir,—l do trust that on this occasion you will
make a special effort to be present with a friend or two,
It is highly desirable, for obvious reasons, that we should
make a “ good show.” Kindly let me know by. Saturday,
13th, how many ticketsyon will require.—Yours faithfully
Eredk. Humphries (Secretary).
Why is a “good show ” especially necessary just
now ?

Old or new coat;

One head, one poll.

One race, one start;
One die, one shake —
One chance, one stake.
One end, one aim—
One limp, one lame
Same truths, same fibs—•
One Parkes, one Bibbs.

Frederick Michael Stokes, one of the
founders of what is now the Sydney Morning
Herald, died at Marrickville, Sydney, on 11th
hist., aged 88 years. With the Rev. Dr. MGarvie
and Ward Stephens as partners, Stokes started
the Herald as a weekly in ’3l; and, 10 years
later, sold jit as a daily to Kemp and Fairfax.

M. S. Hill, the Crump label man of
Sydney, relates that he had in his youth a
large appetite and a Quaker _ master, who, on
one occasion, said to him ; “Friend, it strikes me
that thou art like thy father and thy mother also.”
,l How is that, sir ?” asked the unsuspecting Hill.
Well,” replied the Quaker, “thou eatest very
fast like thy father, and, like thy mother, thou
stayest at it a long time.”

Haddon Chambers (writes our London
representative) has been commissioned by Tom
Thorne to write a new play for the Vaudeville,
which is to be produced before Christmas. Clever
as he undoubtedly is, the general impression is
that it will tax him to the utmost to write a play
to suit a Vaudeville audience. However, he him-
self has no fear on this score, for he is nothing if
not self-reliant.-

faction of knowing that she will get him all to
herself. He is dismissed the affriy and gives up

ariother card again, or insist on their playing
baccarat every night as a proof of her sweet
womanly faith ?
Henry Murray, who has taken upon him-
self to champion literature against the English
critics, is a younger brother of our recent visitor,
David Christie Murray, and is really a clever
fellow, albeit cursed with a temper and a cynicism
which will prevent him from ever being popular.
He is a particular friend of Phil. Robinson.

Ibsen’s portrait as given by a French
litterateur: “ A collar of white beard, an aureola
of white hair round a head of great power; a
little man who takes short steps; speaks little,
but thinks a lot; has_ been a druggist—hence in
all his pieces there is some disease or other. Has
been an actor —hence he will never consent to any
cutting up of any part. Has been hissed in his
own country—hence he lives abroad —mostly at
Munich and Rome. He knows no greater
pleasure than sitting in a cafe for hours without
saying a word to anyone.
The late Edward Broadhurst, Q.C., was
conducting a case in Sydney Supreme Court, J.
B. Darvall being on the other side. A witness
was being examined by him, and a question of age
cropped up. “ What is So-and-So’s age?” asked
Broadhurst. “lam not a good judge of people’s
age,” was the reply. “Well, give a guess,” said
the barrister. ‘ 1 Look at my learned friend and
say which you think is the adulterer of the two.”
Everybody knew Darvall’s proclivities, and the
hit told so well that even the Court laughed.
Deakin’s lecture on orthodoxy, to the
Melbourne University Union, Wilson Hall, last
week, was uncommonly good. All the time he
was sparring at, and thrusting his rapier around,
the most cherished convictions of his hearers. His
late trip to India inspired the address. No other
Victorian is so gifted, with his combination of
politics and culture, but he will do the kootoo to
political orthodoxy, instead of leading Young
Australia. The lecture seems to be a reminder
that he has cast the slough of spiritualism, in
every shape, and is as hard a shell, as Gillies —
or Munro, who prescribes the cold-water cure for
everything.
Parkes, in lately defending his acceptance
of a title, said that before agreeing to take the
“handle*,” he consulted, amongst other distin-
guished personages, Sir John Robertson—leaving
it thereby to be inferred that old Jack Robertson
was altogether disinterested in the matter. Now,
the fact is that Parkes and Robertson were
offered distinctions simultaneously and were not
on speaking terms at the time, nor had they
spoken forayear. But the joy of the pair at the offer
of the Imperial bribes brought them together, and
they actually unbent to the extent of indulging in
a conversation behind the Speaker’s chair on the
subject dearest to their hearts, the result being,
of course, that Jack told Henry to take it, and
that Henry gave similar advice to Jack.

“Old To-Morrow” Macdonald is dead,
aud Canada will never grow another groveller
like him. He was a large and oily man, and his
picture in the evening papers looks like a bird of
prey partly smothered in a blanket. Also he was
a bowling* Tory, a profound wallower before his
beloved Queen, an individual who promised every-
thing to everybody and never gave anything to
anybody, a person of boundless and unfathomable
snuffle, and of such is the kingdom of heaven.
He served his monarch faithfully and died in
harness, for his last public acts consisted in
judiciously faking the electoral rolls so *s to
knock off over 100,000 votes from the democratic
party, and in procuring the rejection of a Bill
providing for the introduction of the One-Man-
One-Yote principle throughout the Dominion.
His friends said that he was a zealous patriot, and
his enemies said that Judas Iscariot and Gehazi
and Ananias all combined weren’t a patch on him
for lying; and the truth was most likely about
half-way between these two assertions. R.I.P,
A light and cheerful American lie is just
now going round the press concerning a gentle-
man who cleaned out all the portable goods,
jewellery, and liquors of a certain Mexican town
by means of hypnotism. He wandered through
the streets, and fixed his evil eye on every citizen
who seemed to have assets, and about an hour
afterwards the inhabitants began to call in and
give him their watches, clothes, furniture, and
brio-a-brao generally. Unfortunately, ten excited
gentleman visited him with a rope and asked an
explanation, and instead of freezing them up with
his baleful optic and taking all their attire off
them, he abandoned his plunder and fled from the
scene. The average hypnotic lie always seems to
come to a lame and impotent ending like this.
If hypnotism were the power it is cracked up to
be the professor of the art would go round and
petrify banks with his mysterious art, and get
forty separate overdrafts ; or he would stun hotels-
with his unholy device, and get free board and
lodging, and gratis liquor; or floor wealthy
capitalists, and make them hand over the accum-
ulated loot. But in real life, instead of this he
hires someseedy person to eat candles and rush
after imaginary rats on the stage, while he collars
the scanty gate-money; and this feebleness of
purpose gives him away. Once, however, them
came to Sydney a hypnotist who aimed at higher
things than these, arid he broke down miserably.
He got a well-known George-street hatter to go*
under the ‘‘influence ” at a public exhibition, and
put him through the customary performance.
Next day a wealthy patron wanted a private
exhibition of the magician’s powers, and he took
him along to the hatter’s shop, and, standing out-
side, commanded the “ subject ” to rush out.
Having once come under the will of the operator
he ought to have been seized on the instant with
a hidden prompting of some sort, and to have
issued forth, dragged by invisible hands ; but he
didn’t issue worth a cent. The hypnotist sawed
the air and made passes, but no one came. He
kept it up for about half an hour, and it began to
rain, and still there were no results. Finally they
;gpt so wet that they couldn’t stand it any longer,
so they went into the shop and inquired for the
patient, and then it transpired that he had left
for Melbourne that morning. The story breaks
off short just there, for no one knows whether the
hatter would have rushed out or not, supposing
he had been present. Most likely he wouldn’t.
We have received the following from “ A
Bluejacket ”
Dear Bulletin, — Early last year you published a few
lines in re a stoker who was discharged from the Victorian
navy because he would not shave his moustache. This
moustache business has cropped .up agaih. Yesterday 1
whs discharged from the Victorian navy by the Naval
Commandant, Captain Mann, because I would, not shave
off my moustache. lam a Victorian by birth, have been
in the navy for 20 months, have borne a good 'character,
have qualified as first-class signalman and seaman-gunner
at the expense of the public, and how have been dis-
charged without a moment’s notice for the above “reason.”
There are about twenty others similarly holding out, and
they are to be discharged, one or two at a time, as men
are got to fill their p-laces. Melbourne, 2nd June, 1891.
From what it knows of Australian public temper.
The Bulletin conceives that this absurd piece of
petty tyranny has only to be mentioned to be
squashed. It would be as reasonable to order
that each man should consent to be ear-marked as
that he should consent to be shaved. Such ukases
may be all very well for the vagrant-department of
gaols.
It is necessary in these days to be always
on your guard against shoddy. No genuine
article comes upon the market now but what
hundreds of unscrupulous traders immediately
commence to imitate and defraud the public by
base and worthless imitations. This has been
more especially the case with Clements Tonic
than with any other article of commerce. The
wonderful therapeutical power of this patent
remedy, has achieved such unprecedented suc-
cess during the last two years, that there is
scarcely a store but what stocks some imitation
for sale to the suffering public, when the
genuine article is demanded ; could anything
be more despicable, is it possible to realise how
debased and jnean a man (who claims to be
educated and a gentleman) must be before he
will trifle with the sufferings of unfortunate
men and women ? And all for the sake of a
few pence extra profit; he knows perfectly
well that he cannot make an article which
would do one tenth the good in diseased
organisms that Clements Tonic does. He
knows that even while he is imposing on his
confiding customer, that he is doing worse than
picking his pocket, for he is betraying the
confidence which the patient places in him. It
is impossible to realise anything meaner or more
despicable. All are not like this ; thank God,
there are honorable men who would scorn to
impose upon their customers, but at the same
time it is absolutely necessary for the public
to be warned against the vampires who pV;ey on
the trust of their patrons, and it is the duty of
every man to expose every case of imposition
or substitution. An honest chemist will hand
you Clements Tonic when you demand it, if he
tries to sell you a substitute,? shun him and
warn your friends against him. Mr. W. Sim-
mons, Sandhurst, Vic., writes under date,
September 19, ’9o:—“Dear Sir,-—For the last
four years I have suffered gri'eat misery from
nervous debility, complicated with liver and
kidney disease, my water was scalding hot and
very scant and muddy. I had sharp shooting
pains all over my body, was very weary, always
tired and languid, with no appetite and fre-
quent attacks of vomiting and headaches. I
rose in a morning more tired than when I went
to bed and was so constipated that I had to
take the most violent cathartics. I consulted
doctors without number and all without benefit,
until my wife persuaded me to use Clements
Tonic. The first three bottles did not seem to
have much effect, but after that I seemed to get
relief daily and a three months course so com-
pletely restored my health, that now I feel as
if I had been born again or translated to an
entirely new sphere of existence. I was never
so well before and feel as strong as a lion and
can perform any reasonable amount of exertion
without fatigue ;• yon can easily understand
bow grateful I am to your wonderful remedy,,
for it- is wonderful that it should have cured
my case which had baffled the best, medical
skill these colonies afford. Long live Clements
Tonic ! say I, others may doubt its. value if
they like, I have proved it genuine.,”

Lozenges and Honey.

The Bulletin sympathises with Gordon-
Cumramg to the extent that he has pot been given
a fair trial. Long after the baccarat-trouble had
been raised, and just before the case came on for
bearing, the Prince of Wales,- as the world knows,
nominated, as a member of thesu'per-exclusive Marl-
borough Club, young Wilson, the scapegoat witness
for the women whom Gordon-Cummingwas sueing
for s’auder. Why'should Wilson, who, with other
members; of hi? family, was, to Wales’ personal
knowledge, just as much on his trial as Gordon-
Cumming, have,, a big social distinction thus
ostentatiously conferred upon him at such a
critical time? Did licit the royal spieler, in per-
forming such an act, deliberately throw his huge
social influence into the scales against Camming?
Did he ; not by that act necessarily intimate
that he believed Camming to be it swindler
and Wilson an innocent ? And ; how looks
the conduct Cof the First' Gentleman of
the Realm in thus committing a cowardly but
unpunishable contempt of court, in view of the
fact that when put into the witness-box he
declared, that lie knew nothing against Gumming
of his ojvn knowledge, but had only found' him
guilty oh hearsay evidence ? The Prince of Wales
has, in this matter, acted a part which would dis-
credit the reputation of a three-card practitioner
or a professional dice-loader.
: # * » V .j 72
Wales’ action in sending his eldest suii'and
Iteir to stop with the Wilsons while the Gordon-
Cumming action was still pending was another
rascally attempt to influence public opinion. The
fact that the engagement to visit had been made ere
the trouble began., is no palliation of such an
offence on the part of the head of British society.
•1 # * *
In a word, Gordon-Cumming has ; so far
as Wales goes, been Welshed.

The Chief-Justice of England, in summing-
up the baccarat case, repeated to the world that
venerable snuffle that there is “but one law alike
for the peasant and the prince.” He omitted to
mention, however, that there are precisely four
thousand and one different ways of administering
that law.

has been the treating of poor Tummy as a military
offender. This is especially comic in the case of
German newspapers, which positively snort at the
notion of a field-marshal gambling with bis subal-
terns. Now, the most devoted adherent ofApyalty
in all; its mock capacities has never sßiolisly
thought of Albert Edward as a real soldier, or
imagined that his fugitive appearances at the head
of a brigade were inoonsisteilt with a pack of
cards and a dice-box in the -crown of his cocked
hat. Neither has the toy-warrior himself asserted

#!# - #
Samuel Blank Blank, of newspaper-can-
vassing fame, appears to have installed himself in
the billet of aide-de-camp 'to Sai’a Bernhardt.
Anyway, Samuel opened the door of her carriage
and saw Sara off, so to speak, as she left
the ‘ Princess’s after the first performance of
“ Cleopatra.” It was midnight. Two rows of
curious lingerers reached from either side of the
stage-doorway to the kerb, and in a commanding
position next to Sara’s brougham, with his bell-
topper neatly balanced on his massive brow, and
a cigar between his teeth, waited Samuel. “She
coraeth not,” he seemed to be saying to himself.
Now and again he would speak to the coachman,
apparently giving instructions as to the proper
way of driving the distinguished burden when she
should be lodged insideA The onlookers \vere
deeply impressed by Samuel’s air of authority,
and whilst some of them whispered that he was
Messrs. Abbey and Grau, others thought he must
be the Cos. to Williamson and Garner. His
masterful repose persuaded all who didn’t happen
to know Samuel that he was there by special
appointment with Sara. He was evidently right
on the job either by invitation or otherwise.
At last Sara emerged from the Princess’
side entrance and tripped across the pave-
ment, followed by a couple of ladies,
With Marc .Antony in attendance. Samuel
turned the handle of the carriage-door, ad-
mitted the ladies, and then shoved his hand
through the open window. Very likely Sara
shook his aggressive paw just to get rid of it—she
couldn’t well help herself, anyway. Marc Antony
managed to make his adieux with some difficulty,
because the other gentleman was pervading the
situation, and then found, just as the carriage
cleared off, that the indomitable Samuel was
grabbing hold of his hand. It is embarrassing
for a stranger in a strange land to be seized upon
in this manner, so Antony tore himself away from
Samuel with a mystified smile and escaped down
the street. Some men might have felt themselves
“left” under the circumstances, but Mr. Blank
meandered in the direction of the tramway-line,
looking radiantly happy. He had come to the
fore on an interesting occasion. Nobody wanted
him there, as far as the present historian could
discover, but Samuel had come all the same. You
can’t keep gentlemen of his temperament in the
background. On the Day of Judgment we shall
expect to discover Samuel offering his hand to the
Lord and remarking that the weather keeps fine.
* # *
The dames of the Austral Salon plume
themselves considerably on the fact that one
participatress in this movement for the higher
. education of woman has a fascinating pair of legs.
At that weary welcoming to Lady Hopetoun the
legs were included in an alleged dance associated
with one of the Tennysonian tableaux, and the
anxiety to see what her ladyship thought of them
was painfully intense. The"whole Salon resembled
a fond mother showing' off her twins. Such
female pride in another girl’s chaste extremities
never was seen before, and every time they kicked
more prominently than usual a dull murmur of
many voices reverberated through the Hibernian
Hall. The worshippers of those golden calves
were saying to one another : “ What a pity His
Excellency couldn’t come U Meanwhile" His
Excellency’s wife was dissimulating her satisfac-
tion at the gentleman’s.absence from a dangerous
position, and wondering how soon the higher edu-
cation of woman will soar above the knee.

“J. A. Packer,” writing in Sydney D.T.
under the heading of “The black list of the
churches,” arraigns two alleged adulterous N.S.
Wales politicians, M‘Rae and Alex. Hutchinson,
as social lepers who should be ostracised by the
electors. It is convenient for persons of the
Packer kidney to omit from their godly little lists
all mention of a certain politician who ran two
wives at once and had a large family by the young
one while the old one was still alive. Such cant-
ing letters only damn “the great N.S.W. Freetrade
party,” and we are quite sure that “the board”
of Sydney D.T, don’t want to do that .

** . *
Old Skipton, just departed from Victoria
to Paradise, was in the ancient days a leading
Southern undertaker, and had a strictly original
way of doing business. On hearing of a death he
made straight for the house, and with tape in
hand and a voice broken with emotion, began
(ex. gr.) : “My dear Mrs. Jones, just heard of
the very sad, sad event (six-foot long). I was just
about to have breakfast (sob) when (three-foot
wide) I got the news (two-foot deep) of the sad
affair’. (Looks well mounted, Mrs. Jones.) What
a fine man (with plumes?) he was, to be sure!
(Did you say at three o’clock?) All flesh is grass
(very well—a hearse and six- coaches), so cheer Up,
Mrs. Jones !”

Ellen Row, of South Melbourne, the othei
day petitioned for adi vorce. Within a webk of hot
wedding, she said, her husband Richard bhcamfe
sweet 0)1 a fascinating neighbour, whom he pro-
miscuously called “Little One ’’and “Patterns/’
and whose premises she (the outraged wife) boldly
entered on one occasion—to discover her hubby
hiding in a cupboard. All this within a fortnight
of the honeymoon ! She forgave the affectionate
Richard, who promised reform, but fell away
from grace again and again. He nearly choked
her, and also threatened her with a revolver in
order to gain possession of a letter for him from
“Fattums,” which she had secured by the or-
dinary devices of jealous women. The Judge
ruled that sufficient cruelty had not been proved.
It is interesting to know that the Victorian defini-
tion of “legal repeated adultery” is “adultery
with more than one person.” So that any Vic-
torian is at liberty to keep a harem cf one and
possibly two, provided lie is not legally cruel to
his legal wife.
# * *
Henry Brett, of Auckland, M.L. (Maori-
land), having an ambition to supply people with
pure literature, illustrated, started a weekly under
the title, The Graphic and Ladies’ Companion.
In a recent issue appeared a picture with an
obscure interpolation, which has simply shaken
Maoriland to its foundations. It is surmised that
the interpolation was the work of the devil. The
worst of it was that the whole edition was in
print ere the trouble was discovered.

and then deposited them with a certain bank.
Next day he called in at the bank and asked for a
loan of the papers, as he wished to make some
extracts from them. The manager, having the
most perfect confidence in him, without demur let
him have the securities—which he at once took
and pawned with another bank. The second bank
registered its mortgage. Then our friend turned
up at the second bank, got a loan of the
deeds as before, and returned them to bank
No. 1. Some time afterwards he turned
up just at the second bank’s closing time,
and sat on the edge of the table in the office
of the manager amiably conversing with that
potentate and wasting much time. Presently the
manager said he had to oatcli his train instanter,
whereupon the speculator : “You had better put
this into the safe handing him a brown-paper
parcel containing something about the size of the
mortgaged and “ borrowed ” deeds. The manager
grabbed the packet, slung it into the safe and
idammed the door kerslap. The deeds weren’t in
the brown-paper parcel, hut that fact wasn’t dis-
covered for quite a long while.
The same smarty raised money from a
bank on mortgage witli the assistance of a photo-
graph of tlie mortgaged property—a house and
ground. Such business, of course, was rather lax,
but then, you see, the bank-manager really
believed in his customer, and had strong prima-
facie reason for thinking that the latter was
in a position far above any small couple-of-
thonsand-pound fraud. To show how trustworthy
the operator was in his financing, it may be men-
tioned that he pulled down the fence in front of
the mortgaged house and had it re-photographed
from a slightly different point of view, and re-
mortgaged it in the same place as another house !
And yet many of his friends say he’s not half a
bad fellow, and not a quarter as black as he’s
painted !

* * *
Agnes Moore, a sweet young bush damsel,
of Huntley (N.S.W.), has Just been favoured by
Acting-Judge Bennett, at Orange District Court,
with £4O damages and costs against Edward
Goodwin Agland for breach of promise of mar-
riage. Edward was foolish enough to reduce
specifically to writing his intentions, thus :
I, Edward Agland, son of .lames Agland, promise to
marry Agnes Moore at any time the father of the said
Agnes Moore may appoint.
As Agnes said that Ted was “the only one she
ever loved or walked with,” not all the contradic-
tions that Edward made, nor his assertions that
the girl’s father and mother frightened him into
signing the above document, in a Gordon-Cum-
ming-like manner, availed with Judge Bennett.
Such is love.

As already hinted by The Bulletin, a 26
year-old Ballarat lady, a graduate of Melbourne
University, is about to marry the venerable Mr.
Henry Halloran, C.M.G., late Under-Secretary
for N.S.W. That makes Henry’s third. We are
getting an epithalamium ready. These poets,
particularly the sonneteers, play fearful havoc
with women’s hearts.

o
snakes which he caught in the Blue Mountains,
and the solemn, soulful Cockney swallows all his
assertions whole. There would be a very poor-
show in this country for a returned Australian
fossil who tried to gibber about the storks and
albatrosses he caught on the Thames, and the
rhinoceros and the great, ferocious winkle that he
hunted in the gloomy forests of Islington; but
the free and independent Briton is a receptacle
for every wild horse-lie that comes along. And
yet he is filled with pity for the ignorance of the
miserable foreigner, because that foreigner doesn’t
know everything.

Accusds from Mildura, Vic., who are com-
mitted for trial, have to be sent to St. Arnaud,
and witnesses who may be required to give three
minutes’ evidence are dragged thither and let go
back again by a round of 1400 miles ! Every
“superior ” criminal from Mildura costs Victoria
at least £IOO to convict or discharge.

The Bulletin does not think that the
N.S.W. Barmaids’ and Waitresses’ Union will
amount to much so far as the first-named is con-
cerned. The stylish barmaid (and all barmaids
try hard to be stylish) will, you may be sure, not
join in the ruck, and will elect to play.a lone hand.
The smart barmaid is always fairly independent
and can change her employer whenever she
pleases.
* * *
Apropos to The Bulletin’s cremation
query, “What is the matter with quicklime?” a
correspondent—“ H.M.”—points out that the use
of quicklime, though it would probably make our
cemeteries last as long again, would simply defer
the nuisance and evil caused by their becoming
too populously stocked. “ H.M.” prophesies that
in another 20 years the Devonshire-street ceme-
tery, Sydney—which has already been closed
several years—will have altogether disappeared,
while the memorial headstones raised by loving
hands will have been utilised by shinbone and
flourbag Aldermen for economical pitching and
paving purposes. He holds the suggested “sani-
tary coffin,” though a much-needed sanitary
reform, to be “ only a compromise; and, in fact,
the insertion of the thin end of the wedge of
cremation pure and simple, which is to burst up
the undertakers and all other churchyard ghouls-
w ho batten on the misery and sorrow of bereaved
ones.”

Consumption v. “Microbe
Killer.”
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co. Gentlemen,—l
wish to add my testimonial to the many others as
I have been suffering for the last twelve months
with lung disease. Have tried all medicines for
the lungs, but all failed to do me good, and I have
been with doctors for a long time; but I got
weaker every day, and my debtor told me my left
lung was no good to me, and that I should never
get any better. I got that weak I believed I could
not live long in such a state, but was recommended
to try your Microbe Killer, and ever since I have
been improving; in fact the change is wonderful,
I have taken one jar and part of another, and I
begin to feel quite strong again. I believe a few
more jars will make a certain cure of me. I
recommend it everywhere I go, as I know the
value of its use, and will be glad to give anyone
information who likes to see me, as I believe it
will cure all diseases; not only that of the lungs,
but all if taken in time. I am yours truly,
Thomas Beeston, Frazer-street, Marrickville.

SPRING and O’CONNELL STREETS.
In the Forties.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ It was the summer of ’4s,’’ started the Captain,
as we gathered closer to hear him. “I was
skipper and part-owner of the old Ariell, just
arrived from Liverpool,” he continued, “and had
that afternoon come up to Melbourne to get money
to pay off my crew. Just after sundown I started
to walk home to my ship, lying at Sandridge pier,
with the money in my great-coat pocket. I kept
both hands in my pockets—one to hold the cash
bags and the other as a sort of balance like. I
went along the Sand ridge-road and a different
place it was then—there were no factories, nor
shops, nor timber-yards, nothing but sand and
scrub. Well, about half-a-mile from town I made
out a man walking on ahead. He was going slow
and I soon caught him. I hailed him with ‘ Good
evening, mate.’ ‘ Good evening,’ says he, and we
walked along together.
“He was not a seafaring man, I perceived, and
as I had never seen him before I’d much rather
not have met him, especially as I had such a lot of -
money aboard. However, I kept my eyes open
and took a good grip of the bags in my pocket.
Just as the lights of Sandridge hove in sight the
man stopped and asked me for a match, saying
his pipe was out. Still keeping my right hand on
my money I searched my pockets with my left,
and gave him one. He took the match but
seemed as though he didn’t care to smoke any
more, for he looked at it and then at me, and
then turned quickly off into the scrub without
saying a word. I was a bit puzzled at the time,
but I thought no more of the man after getting on
board and turning in, glad and thankful to have
my money safe.
Next morning, after I came ashore, I met a
couple of policemen with a man walking between
them handcuffed. He looked up as I came near
and recognised me. ‘ Hallo!’ said I, ‘ that's your
sort, is it ?’ ‘Do you know him?’ said one of the
constables. * Know him ?’ says I. ‘ Well, I met
him only last night on the Sandridge-road.’ ‘ It’s
lucky you don’t know him,’ said he; ‘he’s Jim
Baxter, wanted for the Flinders-street murder.
‘ Yes,’ growled the prisoner, turning to me, ‘An’if
I’d only been sure you hadn’t a pistol in that hand
you kept in your pocket, there might ha’ been
another murder ! Oh, take it down, Jarvie,’
said he, turning to the constable, ‘you can have
the evidence, and be hanged to you.’ "’
“ The next time I saw him he was riding in a
cart down Collins-street, handcuffed, and seated
on a coffin. ”
“ ‘ On a coffin ?’ ” interrupted several voices.
“Yes, they were taking him up to Russell-
street to hang him.”
J.H.G.

CONSULTATIONS.
(“ The Australian Star,” Sydney, June 2, 1891.)
£lOO,OOO SWEEP.
(TO THE EDITOR OF “ THE AUSTRALIAN STAR.”)
Sir,—Will you, through your widely-circulated
paper, inform me and the general public if it is a
fact that “ Tattersall George Adams ” has disposed
of the whole or part of his interest in the well,
known lattersall’s sweep, and that it is now
carried on by a syndicate of Sydney people ? If a
syndicate have it in hand will it be conducted in
the fair and square manner that it was snvmosed
to be when George Adams had it himself.—Yours,
&c - A SUBSCEIBER.
(“ The : Australian Star,” Sydney, June 1891.)
" TATTERSALL’S ” CONSULTATIONS.
In answer to “A Subscriber,” whose letter we
published in our issue of yesterday, we have to
state that upon inquiry we find there is no truth
whatever in the statement that “ Tattersall V’conJ
sultations have been disposed of to a syndicate*
The consultations are still conducted (and
will continue to be as heretofore) under
the direction and sole proprietorship of
“ Tattersall,” and the same principle of
drawing will always be adhered to.
General Post Office,
Sydriey, 15th June, 1891.

waring”: Your long letter contains no new argument.
If you will write, say 40 lines at most, showing why, if a
couple of visiting justices, sitting in secret in a prison,
should have the power to order a man to be flogged, they
should not also be given the power to have him hanged,
we will print it. Also, flogging being just as great a
degradation to a citizen as the scaffold, you might let us
know why, while no citizen can be hanged without going
before a jury, a couple of mere blustering, ignorant magis-
trates, appointed only because they happen to be some-
one’s uncle or cousin, should by law have the power to
order an accused man to be flogged right away? For our
own part, we regard so undemocratic a law with unutter-
able horror and loathing, and cordially detest as brutal
tyrants all Australian members of Parliament and all
other persons who have ever taken part in making or re-
newing such laws. “ Mine ” : Better the “ blue-blooded ”
Tory than the pompous “Liberal.” Better the Earl of
Jersey than Mr. Henry Broadhurst, of the white waistcoat
and black broadclothA. VV.Y.”: Many thanks
“Ames W. ’: Yes, some young Australians do speak
through their noses. If they don’t, pray tell us how do
they manage to say “kaow” for “cow,” and “haouse ”
for “house” ?....“ Cara ” : Not unique. A burst of
laughter in a murder case is incongruous, but very usual,
because the defending advocate knows it always turns in
favour of the culprit.
Let the English call the Australians “Our
Cousinsi” Uo longer. Australia is the nephew of
its British Uncle.
The Queensland strike is over. It is the
last of great Australian strikes ; henceforth the
ballot-box. Never again will the associated work-
men cut their noses to spite their faces.
“Traveller” (loq.) : “An extraordinary
barbaric relic is the trap-door which a publican
is allowed to open to roll in his beer-barrels. Here
you have a narrow and crowded street, with all
the foot-passengers tutned into the roadway.
From the newspaper reports it would
appear that when Mr. Higgs, of the N.S.W.
Trades and Labour Council, feels very disgusted
he “gets a pain in the stomach.” There are,
therefore, three alternatives before Mr. Higgs :
either to boiler-plate his conscience so that he will
never be disgusted, to electro-plate his stomach,
to avoid the results of disgust—or to keep out of
Parliament.
The Christchurch (M.L.) licensing com-
mittee, composed entirely of teetotallers, is going
as near prohibition as the law allows, and has re-
fused many licenses. A teetotal licensing-bench
is as big a s windle as a Melbourne honorary bench,
which, in nine cases out of ten, is merely a con-
spiracy against law and justice, and in favour of
some pal of its own.
The “ Cup ’’Blend a marvellous tea in the cup.
The re-appearance of M'Elhone on the
N.S.W. political warpath revives the memory of
the good old day when he stood and was elected
for East Sydney. The gentle John was then a
warm, not to say torrid, supporter of the Dailey-
Stuart Government, and Dailey wrote for him his
address to the electors; The “shirtsleeved
Caesar ” was in that particular the most good-
natured soul alive. His facile pen was at all
times at the service of any acquaintance who was
in difficulties over a speech or an article, a
characteristic known and availed of by all sorts
and conditions of men, from Governor Loftus up
to the budding journalist who wanted to round off
an obituary or give a classical turn to a subleaderor
a review. However, Mac. couldn’t be comfortable
for long on the right of the Speaker. A few days
or weeks of the session drove him to his only
possible resting-place the Opposition benches
where he foregathered with A. G. Taylor and
became a thorn of a very bad kind in the side of
the Government. Something had to be done, and
the Government put up Dan O’Connor to do it.
Dan’s speech, needless to say, was wordy and
rambling, but no one who heard it is likely to
forget the unexpected and startling power of its
conclusion. The man he was attacking positively
cowered and grew livid under the sting of the
blistering peroration that came to a merciless
climax in a discharge of all the venom
which could be concentrated into the two words,
“political Quilp! ” Now, the polished, apt
brutality of these words it would be vain to try
to make properly clear to anybody who has not
lived in New Soxrth Wales. It is almost unneces-
sary to add that the manuscript of the peroration
in Dan’s notes was in the handwriting of W. B.
Dailey. The little comedy didn’t end there. The
future Postmaster-General himself became discon-
tented with something or other—the postal policy
of the Goverment, perhaps—-and found himself
in his turn compelled to cross the floor. Dailey,
who represented the Government in the Upper
House, had thereupon the misfortune to do some-
thing which caused the blocking of O’Connor’s
pet Sydney Corporation Bill, and the air grew
black with thunder once again. The last act was
short and sharp. A furiom onslaught in the
Assembly, in which Dan made a graceful affusion
to Dailey’s parentage, evoked a withering sen-
tence or two which, though it didn’t deign to be a
reply, would have made even a laughing jackass
wilt and droop and hold his tongue. A Sydney
pressman, by the way, has a story which gives
Dan away pretty badly over that same speech
against M'Elhone. When it was over, he (the
pressman) went downstairs from the gallery to fill
in a word which he was not quite sure he had
correctly caught. “ What was that last expres-
sion you used, Mr. O'Connor—* political whelp ’ ?”
“No,” said Dan, turning up the manuscript,
“‘political Quilp.’ Bui put ‘whelp,’ it's abetter
word /”
A “Government House Young Man."
Dear Bullock Team, I take exception to
two adjectives in your “Plain English ” of June
6th. Before coming to them I must tell you how
I read The Bulletin.
First. —Headings, in order to see whether there
is any stuff for me. Excerpt from Bush Book-
shonary : —Stuff : Cash, money ; 2, medicine ; 3,
vide other lexicographers.
Next.—Pictures, i.c., drawings. N.B.—l’m
certain that Ashton has a wife.
Thirdly.—Plain English. N. Optissimc. This
is my present dart.
Fourthly, and lastly, as we say inth epoopit. —l
go through ads., leaders, pars., Sappho, and all
usque ad finem.
To get back to the scent, just as my dogs
Snooks and Minnie do (best dogs in N.S.W.), I
call your attention to No. 3. Up to date all has
been padding to you, but for the multitude it is
explanatory matter.
, Now, you rise the word “ Scotch ” in, to say the
least of it, an equivocal manner. Is not a Scotch-
man as good as anyone else and a d d sight
better? I refer to the words: “A Government
House young man with merely a Scotch letter of
introduction.” Hang you, I’m Scotch.
To continue —“Any gented boarding-house.”
A boarding-house can’t be genteeh and genteel is
not a genteel word. It’s a Styx of a word.
Still I perceive the little “ granum salis” that
pervades your article : “Trades Hall and Govern-
ment House.”
As I have advanced in years I have wondered
why I was always looked upon with more favour
than my two elder brothers. At first I thought it
was because I was clever; and that’s another con-
temptible word.
But my brother Jack Was, perhaps, the] best
mathematician of his day and ran the blockade
three or four times as an engineer when the steam-
kettles of his boat Were close-up dangerous.
And Jim was a most moral young man. He,
like me, was a soldier, and, from what I have
heard, a good onei He was not killed in action-
on the contrary, lie was murdered by leaky huts
and cold at the Curragh, where he had to make
room for My Lords Champagne and Stomjack,
who were then in the first blaze of their devilry
(good enough fellows, both of them).
But I, the third, combining the mathematics of
my elder with the neat manners (not morals) of
my second brother, was the show- boy. And why ?
I’ll tell you. Being of a Tory family, unfor-
tunately they taught me logic. Consequently I
sneered at the petty grandiosity of instituted
customs and was teckoned “ verra neer daft.”
When I sang “ We’ll hae nae king but Charlie,”
I substituted “Barley.” But God gifted me with
memory and reason. Whilst the late Lord Advo-
cate was studying in his room I was looking for
nests and whatever else is to be found “within a
mile o’Edinboro’ toon,” and—became a soldier.
Then entered Satan into my heart and I allowed
myself to swagger, with sword and sabretasche
and jingly spurs, down Waterloo Place even unto
the palace of St. James. From that day dated
my ruin ; Iliad become “a Government House
young man.” And still I sneered and was put
down as peculiar (good word that). But my
people — my people because my brothers died —a
race who, holding their heads above the local
aristocracy, because they cuild sway the electorate,
and respected by the workers, because they em-
ployed a hundi’ed men in the most noble of
all charities, the making of whisky ; these people,
my people, caught the infection of toadyism and
laid up to me, the Government House young man.
Here I will say that the common people liked me
because I was a “ deevil for huntin’, an’fushin’,
an’ rid in’, an] the lassiesand therefore, thusly I
am a Republican and a sinner, waiting peacefully
for that time when Republics, Commonwealths
and Royalty will cease to interest
Your very attached friend,
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
■ P.S, —Whateverßudyard Kipling, Miss Brad-
don and Derrick Vaughan may do, I never
re-write. So you may puddle and cradle this off;
but I think it’s good, because it’s true. —S. le W.

A MOST EXTRAORDINARY STATE-
MENT.
A MODERN MIRACLE.
To the Editor of the Wellington (Salop)
Journal.
Dear Sir, —In your paper of October 20 I saw
the account of what 1 considered a most extra-
ordinary statement, wherein “Mrs. Mary Ann
1' os ter, of 48, Greenfield-terrace, Gateshead, had
be 3ii cured of rheumatism in the limbs in 15
minutes by using St. Jacobs oil.” Now, this
statement of itself seems incredible, but when the
article stated, further, that “for a long time her
legs had been so stiff that she could not sit up in
bed,” that “the ligaments of her joints seem to
have grown together,” and that “her case was
considered hopeless ” it seemed a miracle Which I,
determined to investigate. I accordingly called
on Mrs. Foster for the purpose of learning the
truth. Judge of my surprise when that lady told
toe that every word was true. She said for
months previous to using the oil she had been
confined to her bed suffering the most excruciating
agony night and day, but that in 15 minutes after
the application she experienced relief from pain,
and in less than a week she was up and about the
house —a well woman. Mrs. Foster says this was
more than 12 months ago and she has not had
any return of the disease. She is as well and
strong as she ever was in her life, and (naturally)
recommends St. Jacobs oil to everybody—in fact,
is a living advertisement for St. Jacobs oil. I
hand you these facts solely for the benefit of the
public, believing that a remedial agent possessing
such wonderful power to conquer pain and relieve
suffering should be made known everywhere. I
am glad to see that your journal, as well as the
press generally, is taking up the matter, which is
quite right.
I remain, yours respectfully,
THOMAS HUTCHKISS.
4G, Napier-street, Newcastle-on-Tyne.
B EYOND COMPETITION.
The Waterbury Watches take front rank as
“the timekeepers of the day.” They are not
“ English levers ” made in Geneva, and puffed
into notoriety to enforce sales and ensure disap-
pointment. The factory, which is now turning
out over half a million of these watches per
annum—a production larger than that of any
other watch company in the world—is still unable
to keep pace with the demand, and has now
extended its operations into the very heart of the
cheap watch-making territory on the continent
of Europe, where it successfully competes with
these “English levers” on equal terms. These
are no idle boasts, but stubborn facts, and we
challenge the refutation of these assertions. The
Waterburys (series E, J, and L) are only nickel-
plated watches sold at a low figure, but they get
their reputation from live purchasers and not the
owners of dead stock. They are far more reliable
timekeepers than half of the jewelled-all-over-
patent-halfcock-go-as-you-please-back-action-hair-
trigger - compensated - lock - stitch - burglar - baits
made in Switzerland and Germany, and sold as
English levers. The Waterbury'Watches have
nothing to fear from ridicule, no dread of ex-
posure. They are simply what they claim to be,
and defy competition.
These World-famed and time-tried watches may
be obtained from any respectable dealer or store-
keeper thoroughout the colonies. Prices : Series
E, the old favourite, 13s fid ; Series J (gents’ size),
and L (ladies size), the new shortwinding, stem-
winding, and setting marvels, 22s fid each. No
watch sold as a Waterbury is genuine without the
word Waterbury and the interwoven letters W.
W. Cos. printed in fine type upon its dial face.

A Curious Reminiscence.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Of all the bloomin' awful things, the awfullest I’ve
knowed
In the flye-and-sixty years I’ve bin alive,
Took place at Paddy Doolan’s on the old Jerilda-road, -' l
’Way back in sixty-four or sixty-five.
Old Doolan had a handy man, a useful sort o’ chap,
But a real, right-down tiger for his ruin ;
An’, one day, bein’ tipsy, why he tumbles from a trap,
And cracks his skull, an’ goes to “ kingdom come.”
Well, they sends an’ tells the trooper, an’ the trooper
rides across,
An’, says he, “I’ll have to let the 1 Crowner ’ hear ;”
So they stretches out old Jerry : iij the place he used to
doss—
A tumble-down old shanty at the rear.
I was trampin’ down from Bulga, an’ had just run out of
. grim ;
Rainin’, too, till every rag on me was soaked ;
So,,you bet, 1 wasn’t sorry to pull-up at Doolan’s pub,
On the evenin’ of the day that Jerry croaked.
Well, I’d had my bite o’ tucker an’ a glass or two o’ beer,
An’ was sittin’ there a-flllin’ of my pipe,
When in comes Mad Macarthy an’ Long Jim of Bundaleer,
An’, my word ! hut they was well upon the swipe.
Two strappiri’ big six-footers, an’ as strong as bullocks,
both ;
An’ ripe for any devilment, as well;
The man that interfered with ’em, you just might take
your oath,
Stood a pretty lively chance of catchin’ hell.
Well, they drank an’ laughed an’ shouted, an’ they swag-
gered an’ they swore,
-, Till Macarthy took a notion in his head
That ,ttiey’d make old Jerry drunker than he’d ever bin
before;
“ Yez can’t do that!” says Doolan—“ Jerry’s dead !”
“Dead drunk, you mean!” says Jimmy. “No,” says,
Doolan, “ no, begob !
He’s as dead as he can be —without a lie!
For he tumbled out of Thompson’s trap, right fair upon
his nob.”
Says Macarthy, “ Come, old man, that’s all my eye I”
“Bedad !” says Pat, “then take a light an’go yerselves to
see.
Sure he’s lyin’ in the shed forninst the gate.”
So my nobles gets a candle an’ they tips a wink to me,
An’ out they goes to see if it was straight.
Well, sir, back they comes directly an’ a-laughih’ fit tc
split,
And behind ’em Mother Doolan cryin’ “ Shame !”
And well she might, for there, between the pair of ’em,,
was it —
The clay that used to answer Jerry’s name !
With its head a-hangin’ forward and its legs a-dragguT
loose,
You can bet it was a dreadful sight to see!
And Istarted up to stop’em, but says Doolan, “Where’s:
the use!
They could smash a dozen chaps like you an’ me !”
So they humped their fearsome burden to a corner of the 1
bar,
And propped it on a cask agin the wall; ' •
“What will Jerry drink?” says Jimmy, and Macarthy
says, “Three-star!
For we won’t be mean with Jerry—damn it all!”
Well, sir, Doolan fills three nobblers, an’ Macarthy collars:
one,
And slaps it down before the senseless clay;
Then they bobs their heads to Jerry, an’ says they: “ Old
man, here’s fun ! ”
And punishes their liquors right away.
Then’twas, “Fill’em up again, Pat —fill’em right up to'
the brim !”
Till they’d swallered half-a-dozen drinks a-head;
Then Macarthy stares at Jerry’s glass, an’ then he stares'
at Jim—
-1 “ By the Lord ! ” says he, “ old Jerry must be dead!
“ For it’s five-an’-twenty minutes he has had his pizeiv
there,
And he’s never tried to touch a bloomin’ drop !
So ■you’re right for once, old Doolan ! Have a drink, an’ -
let us square.
For it’s nearly gettin’ time for us to hop.”
So they humped old Jerry back again to where he wis
before—
Mot her Doolan still a-scoldin’ them in vain ;
Then they staggers to their horses that was stand in’ at
the door,
An’ gallops off like madmen through the rain.

Extract from Carlyle’s “Transit of Czar
Peter ” in his “ Frederick ’’—showing the state off
Russian society in 1717 :
Most of the four hundred so-called ladies of the suite—
mere German serving-maids—had in their arms a baby in
rich dress, and if you asked “Is that yours?” they
answered, “ The Czar did me the honour.” One President
Cocceji and several official gentlemen had to present a
complimentary address. On entering they found his
Czarish Majesty standing between two Russian ladies, one
of his arms round the neck of each ; in which position he
remained all the time Cocceji spoke.
Henniker Heaton high falutes on his
penny ocean postage scheme in the “ Colonial
Year Book” To melt the frozen streams of
sympathy, to strike the shackles from the numbed
limbs of commerce, to annihilate the chilling sense
of distance, to mass the scattered forces of our
race and foster the proud Imperial sentiment-
such aims may well awaken our enthusiasm,
strengthen us against ignorant or interested oppo-
sition and nerve us for the final effort, which shall
add a new and precious privilege to the inalienable
birthright of Englishmen ? ”
The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson, lies:
before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while transferrable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away or
given as security, while bearing interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 6 months, 6
par cent.; 3 months, 5 percent. But special rates
can be procured fop longer or shorter periods.,
Tlie Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years, 9i
per cent. ; 12 months, 8J per cent. ; 6 months, 7g ( ,
per cent.; 3 months, 5£ per cent., and during the
last four year§ a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
to all depositors in addition to the current rates on
their deposits;' This is the only Bank in Austra-
lasia that thus divides its profits.

Dear Bulletin, There is surely one
: reform which the Labour party, as soon as it feels
its feet in the various Australian legislatures,
!should undertake, viz., a day-Parliament. The
man of leisure rises at 1 p.m., and would not be
i ready for the House before 4 or 4.30 ; the business
man (the good old member who was so glad to
“serve his country without pay from purely
patriotic motives”) wants the day for making
bargains and gives the fag-end of his time in the
■evening to conserving the privileges of his caste.
A day-Parliament, where men work with clear,
fresh brains, unfuddled by the booze which always
accompanies late hours, is the Parliament for
Labour. The Parliament of South Australia, un-
questionably the most democratic of all the Aus-
tralian States, has always met at 2 p.m., but the
proper hour for a paid democratic Parliament
should be 11 a.m., if not 10. There is one small
side issue to the subject which will appeal to the
spirit of fairness in every Labour member. The
present system allows the newspaper-proprietors to
work their reporters from 10 a.m. to 4 p.m. in
‘Courts, deputations, meetings, and general work,
:and then to work them from 4 p.m. to midnight
■or 2 a.m. in Parliament.—Yours, &0., X.Y.Z.
In addition to the two land-grant railway B
iin Western Australia on a large scale, there are
several minor lines constructed on the same
principle. The owners of the Jarrahdale timber
■concessions in the south have built their own
irailway, and recently Messrs. Millar Bros,
■obtained the concession to build a line from
Albany to Eorbay—l6 miles —for a grant of 2000
acres per mile. Another proposal has just been
■entertained by the Government of a more liberal
nature. Mr. E. Keane, M.P., contractor for the
Midland Railway, has agreed to construct a line
of 28 miles in length, connecting a very valuable
timber area (owned by the contractor) with the
■city. He demands no concession of land, but will
allow the Government to take over the line at a
valuation at the end of seven years.
A Victorian paper anounces with an air of
pride that a Trentham farmer has five men at
work digging potatoes, whose joint ages—the
men/’ not the potatoes—amount to 348 years,
making an average of about seventy years, each.
The country editor is considerably inflated over
this circumstance, but there doesn’t seem to be
much cause for congratulation in the’ fact that the
used up Australian patriarch has to go digging in
his extreme old a£e in order to earn a miserable
pittance that will just keep him alive. If the
writer had stated that these five Individuals had
retired from business on five separate annuities
provided by a system of national insurance, and
that some younger individuals were digging
potatoes for them, there,-i would be a lot
more satisfaction in the anecdote.
A Yankee named Hurlbert (his real name
is said to Be HurKhU), editor for iriariy years of New
York World/ ripw proprietor 'of tries’ Central
News Press-Association 1 , ikin tibub'le on account
of a hkrd-'sweating female --who lately brought
against him an action in London for' brea'ch-of-
promise. The .lady states that she, met Hurlbert
(a snob who poses in London as an Englishman,
and ingratiates, himself with the old, and new
nobility by writing anti-Irish pamphlets]! in an
omnibus, whpn they loved each othpr with a deep
and sudden affection ; and thereafter fie led her
astray under promise of marriage, arid for several
months she lived with him as his friisfress. She
has a nuftiber of letters (riiostly of a hideonsly-
indecent character) in what', if riot his own hand-
writing, is utterly therefrom,
and with his signature attached ; and she can point
out the house where they lived together; and a host
of vociferous witnesses are prepared to identify
both Hurlbert and herself as the occupants of the
house; and moreover the man who took the place
used Hurlbert’s name as well as his head and face,
and his clothes, and his handwriting. On the
other hand Hurlbert makes affidavit that he
is as innocent as a new-laid egg; that he was
never in that omnibus—that he doesn’t know
the lady—and that a fiend in human shape, who
looks just like him, wrote the letters, and took
the house, and perpetrated the whole proceeding,
and that this monster is now snuggering
in his loathsome den over the ruin he
has accomplished. He asserts that this
particular reptile was formerly his secretary,
and that he and the secretary cannot be distin-
guished apart, and that the bounced employe
imitates his hand-writing better than he can do it
himself. His secretary, in fact, looks more like
Hurlbert than Hurlbert does, and it takes the
latter all his time to prevent.this infamous char-
acter seizing upon his office, and taking over
his wife and family, and his home, and his bank-
account, and firing the real owner into the street
as an impostor. If the duplicate person chose to
rush the office some morning and run the business
before Hurlbert got down, the latter is convinced
that it would be all over with him, for his un-
principled shadow would not only look like him,
but would write leading articles and sign cheques
in his hand, and swear in his voice, and wear his
clothes; and Hurlbert himself, who had owned a
valuable property a moment before, would be
cleaned out in an instant, and wouldn’t own any-
thing or be anybody. And simply by reason of
this fiendish resemblance he can’t find anybody
who ever saw the impostor, for everybody who
saw him took him for Hurlbert himself, and
therefore this harassed individual has nothing
but his own hard swearing to defend on. Even
that, too, doesn’t amount to much, for it may be
the other man who is swearing all the time, the
two swear so much alike, that even an affidavit
can’t disentangle them. Hurlbert got the verdict,
as the lady failed to prove that he had promised
her marriage; but either she or he should be
heard of shortly in connection with a first-class
perjury prosecution.

Dear Bulletin, —In the Queensland in-
terior heavy rains usually occur about the
latter end of February or March When the summer
has cooled off a bit. Sometimes, however, the
rain sets in right on top of the hottest weather.
These peculiar conditions seem to suit the sand-
flies who crop up in billions, So that for‘a week or
two nothing else is talked of, and man arid beast
suffer alike. They drive the wildest Station-
horses into the stockyard where fires are kept
burning all day long. The cattle become one
restless moaning mob vainly seeking refuge in
numbers; and even the stockmen are maddened
by the festering bite, although they show but a
few vulnerable inches of skin. It was in one of
the worst sandfly times that Augustus 'Bigg
Neucholmondeley drew up on the banks of Sandy
Creek and flapped himself smartly with a gum-
bough as he looked across the turgid surface of its
waters to the other bank’ where the track shook
itself free from the water. The current was not
swift but the creek was broad—very broad—a
hundred yards, at least, and ’Gus felt; that it was
necessary to strip in order to get over. He was
a strong swimmer and there was a good get-out on
the other side. So he dismounted, started a fire,
and under the protection of the smoke undressed.
He then tied his clothes into a ball and strapped
it on the top of his head. Then he passed
his belt through the tags of his boots and
slung them round his neck. He then drew the
old horse into the smoke, and as quickly as the
thumps of bare heels could do it urged him into
the stream. He had made a mistake. The muddy
current he had taken to be at least ten feet deep
scarcely covered his horse’s fetlocks. It was a
flat sandy bed right across, not a foot at the deep-
est part. If Augustus had had any sense he
would have gone back again into the smoke, but
he kept on thinking he would be into the deep
water every step. Old M‘Kenna, who happened
to meet him on the other side, thought for a
minute he had come on a mad blackfellow. Rigg
was dusky with sandflies and dancing with pain
and itch, rolled in the sand, while—well, old Mac.
said he didn’t know where Augustus could have
learnt it, as he was only a nevv-chum.—M.
