816 Macquarie-strcet North, Sydney, Jane 17,1891.
Mr Dkar MoorabisbA,—
I am told—though I scarcely believe it — that three
Sydney society girls, aided and abetted by a matron r f
the frisky Variety, hither surprised a handful of men
Whom they (in the matron’s name) invited to a theatre-
party recently, and champagne-supper’d afterwards in a
private room at a public cafC ! Of Course, a feed is the
only possible way of rounding off a theatre-party, and
When it happens at a private house nobody can find fault
with the arrangement. But for a bevy of girls (everybody
was fully aware that matron was a polite myth When the
bill came in) to " shout' 1 a midnight spread to four favoured
male friends, reads something worse than “ Uncon-
ventional,” doesn’t it ? And apropos tb such things, it i a
whispered that a “set” of rather reckless “ sassiety
girls” make the pace Very hot at times when the
chaperone is asleep. At a recent dance, for instance, one
fair daughter of a well-known Sydney ihan amused her
partner in a conservatory interval by smoking two
cigarettes at the same-time, and blowing the smoke down
her nostrils.
Still the female suffragists rage, and still the hapless
being who ventures to disagree with them is indignantly
asked—" Ought not a Woman ” (With such a big W) “to
have a word in the making of those laws which she is
expected to keep?” But the question is—Are women
supposed to keep the laws as stringently as meh, and, if
not, will they ever be expected to do so? Certainly the
punishment which one of our sex gets when she breaks one
of them (a law, not .a man), doesn t indicate that, in prac-
tice at least, Woman is on the same plane as Man. As a rule
of thumb, every female inhabitant tries not to steal her
neighbour s goods or poison him—but if she does, what
sort of retribution overtakes her? They don t(nowadays)
flog women, and, as a rule, they don't hang them, and a
barrister once told me that he has seen a pardoning gleam
in the eye of the sternest judge at the slightest evidence
of softness or “womanliness” in a female criminal.
Looked at thUsly—as favoured beings to whom only half
the law is applicable, women ought not to be enfranchised
sotely because they have no say “In the laws which they
are expected to keep,” but which they very, very seldom
feel in their full severity.
Taking them "by and large,” as sailors say, the
Women’s Rightists of Sydney are weird human produo.
tions who do not hesitate to boldly advertise their
qualities of mind and soul, Which disappear when
alluded to. Who was it said that when you began to
boast of virtue it was gone ? But the vast mob of women
who yearn to insert their finger Into the Parliamentary
pie, and who disguise what is only another phase of female
inquisitiveness under the aspect of a desire to right the
“ wrongs” of their fellow-women—are not the angels of
mercy they advertise to be. If woman, as a class, is so
jealous of her liberties, why are the majority of evening
shoppers arrayed in skirts and bonnets? Of course the
poor soul is sympathetic. “What a pity they don’t let
you go home at six !” she says comfortably to the haggard
■hop-girl as she orders down still another bale of rags at
9.30.
Lady Jersey does not seem to be an enthusiastic slip,
porter of the enfranchised female. I think it was at New-
castle that our social apex refused to express her views,
yet gave them by saying: “ There is a great deal of work
that must be done by women only. If they add to this
all the work of men, they must either do double duty,
which is not good for anyone, or neglect the tasks which
are more distinctly their own.” In other plainer words—
“ Keep on rocking the cradle and let the old man get his
coat torn off at the ballot-box,” is “ M. E. Jersey’s ” advice
to her temporary subjects. lam not disposed to quarrel
with it.
The first Jersey June “At Home” was quite a new
thrill, even for the jaded Government House haunter, for
Dutch tops and conversation were the diversions provided
to the dull thud of a concealed brass band; but as the
party had rather grown out of playing with tops the
game was voted deadly slow and chin-music had it all its
own way. The dressing was good, though there was a
preponderance of dowagers—in regard to whose perpetua l
presence at all Government House shivoos it has been
fervently wished that vice-regality would gently shelve
these withered monuments of the past and give the
coming woman a show. Lady J. (who looks best in rich
warm colours) wore ivory satin and very special diamond
and pearl ornaments; her two girls were in red pongCe
■potted white; Miss Stephen in shrimp-pink; Miss
Burdett-Smith, white bengaline trimmed with jet; Mrs-
Vanderveer-Greene in white silk, ciel-blue chiffon and
ostrich tips. There were many black gowns-the
prettiest being worn by Mrs. Chipman who combined
black and gold most effectively.
By the way, at this gay and festive whirl, great interest
"was taken in an audible fight between a well-known
Sydney man and his wife as to whether it was or wasn’t
the caper to pump-handle the hostess on taking leave—
he, being a man and merciful, contending that it Was
Superfluous while the lady (it is needless to remark that
WOn the battle) insisted on the tired farewell squeeze.
They are not the first married couple who have sulked
home over this very interesting point of etiquette, nor
will they be the last, since nearly everybody is deeply
agitated aiid bothered as to what is really the correct thing
lo db, and nobody seems to be able to give anybody a
pointer about it. But a society butterfly of the
airiest description, Who would sooner die than dogs-ear
the wrong corner of her visiting card, kindly gave me her
plan of campaign on these occasions: “If I leave early
I slip away ns quietly ns I can, but if I wait until the
break-up of the affair, I make a point of bidding my
hostess ‘good night.’” But, reverting to the connubial
divergence of opinion which amused everybody so, isn't
it rather significant of how very “ occasional ” is the
veneer of local society—two people audibly squabbling as
to what is “ manners?”
Aow that the Jerseys have led off With a'n evening
entertainment, consisting chiefly of parlour-games, it is
on the cards that their aggravating pastimes will have a
tun, and that the dancing man will hang his pumps on a
nail and cultivate a passion for hunt-the-slipper, and
the dancing girl get up an enthusiasm for “magic
fnusic” instead of the deux temps. But so long a s
society keeps strictly to “ silence and fun,” as playbills
Say, then everything will be right as rain—l only quail
when I think of those dreadful parlour pastimes which
hall for a dertain amount of mental effort. Imagine a
typical Potts Pointer lured into that lively game, “ A
ship ckme to Liverpool laden with ” A, B, C, and so on.
I can almost hear the bejewelled creature, as it comes to
S, pompously announcing that the vessel carried
“ cigars.”
Since the Melbourne suburbs have been metamorphosed
into citiek, each little suburban mayoress has commenced
“ teceiving ” in the local Town Hall with the lynx-eyed
reporter of the Suburban Snooks panting after the clothes
of the grubby little assemblage of people Who turn up at
the uSual weird refreshments in on’C corner. There is a
perfect mania among women now to stand wherever they

The matin6e operetta, “ Prince and Pedlar,’ 1 performed
by the Fort-street Public School childreh at Her Majesty’s
on 13th inst in the presence Of Lady Jersey, Mrs. J. H.
Carruthers, and a three-thousand attendance, had only
one item which really made the andier.Ce open its eyes,
and that was the apparition of a pale, small girl With gas-
pipe arms and legs who advanced to the footlights and
whistled very sweetly, but in rather bad time, the Waltz-
song from “ Pirates;” and in response to a thumping
encore an Arditi waltz; It was the prettiest whistling I
have ever heard, and it was amusing to see how the boy 4
on the stage and in the circle craned their necks listening
to every trill that came from her funny; putsed-up little
mouth. That is the worst cl whistling, it creases the lips
so—and probably when this little siflleuse grows up with
what is commonly'called an absolutely unkissable “pipe
mouth,” she Will be sorry she ever did it* Soule of the
Robber choruses were good, and dressing and mounting
were all right,'but everybody sang too slowly, and the
soloists' voices weren’t strong enough to fill the theatre-
I was pleased to see how awkwardly nearly all the per-
formers behaved, for anything is better than brazen
kplomb in a child, though one fat maiden with thick ankles
and a husky voice, attitudinised and sang like quite an old
hand. There were no dresses worth a glance in the d.c.
Lady jersey, presented with a beautiful cream-tinted
bonnet-bouquet, wore pale-blue woollen with panel and
trimmings of bide moire feather enhanced by bands or
jewelled trimming, small bonnet in black and canary
velvet and jet; her three daughters in biscuit silks and
big leghorn hats and feathers, and the small scion as usual
in a sailor suit. Mrs. Carruthers, who occupied a seat in
the vice-regal box, wore black, with a fetching little
bonnet in black lace and jet. Secretary Goschen looked
after the party with his mouth open, as usual.-
At an Ashfield marriage the other day the bridesmaids
came in for all the delicate little attentions usual on such
occasions through a crowd of girl friends at the church
mistaking their carriage for that of the bride. They
tied old shoes to the handle and threw rice after the
blushing damsels, who, of course, didn’t know what to do
with the showering grain. The bride and groom escaped
scot free.
After all the excitement oyer it, the real race-scene in
II County Fair,” at Her Majesty’s, though a very
thrilling spectacle, is not, in my opinion, half so interest-
ing as the comedy-drama itself, which is a complete get-
away from the virtuous heroine and the bold, had baronet
sort of play. Comedienne Sadie Stringham plays Miss
Abbey for all that amusing Yankee is worth, and her
fall downstairs at the end of the first act convulses even
the d.o. Also her “business,” as she probes with a
broom-handle for the ever-dreaded “man under the
bed,” must appeal to every old maid in the house who has
ever jumped at her own shadow after nightfall, or
calmly looked under her virtuous couch in the hope that
she would find a man there. Watts-Phillips had all the
gowning in this life-like show—her first array of King’s
crimson with neck-folds of white mull; her second dress
of dove-grey with a pale-pink sun-bonnet; her third, of
pale-pink muslin very daintily cut; and her fourth of
cream delaine trimmed with sap-green and pink reversible
satin ribbon, all looked especially fresh considering the
every-day prevalence of dingy winter clothes. By the
way, the evening I witnessed “County Fair,” pianist
Ernest Hutcheson occupied the centre box with a party
of ladies, and what pleased me with him was, that when
the house clapped the orchestra for a very pretty
rendering of some of Bellini’s music, Mister (or rather
Master—he is so young and boyish-looking) Hutcheson
also gently applauded.
Melbourne, seemingly, has not yet discovered that
La Bernhardt paints her gums, but she does, and her
little pot of gum-grease costs fifteen-and-six. Now, in
case Society wants to trump her trick, I might as well
add that you can’t get the salve in Australia—as yet—
though the great living example of Sara is sure to create
an immediate demand, since society—regarded in the
the light of a lovely fresco—does not leave out the finer
touches. Indeed, on a river-steamer recently I sat
alongside an elaborate face-artist who had actually painted
a poetic blue line—to represent a vehi—on her white-
washed temple. “A good painting,” whispered a saw-
bones friend of mine, “but she doesn’t know her
anatdmy.”

papers. I was glad, however, to see that he flaUnted a
big red nosegay the size of a cheese-plate. It suited him,
in a Swinburnian sense.

When I was budding in my innocence and youth I was
about as Credulous as they are made now-a-days—but
since that halcyon period I have linearthed so many
shams that hdw at the age of, well—well, look at my like,
hess at the top of this column if you want to know- I do
nothing but turn up deceptions masquerading as every-
thing but What they really are. The last whited sepulchre
that I have bumped against is the lovely legend—“ Dottie’s
trousseau comes from England- every rag of it, from the
gown to the garters.” Only the other day a pretty bride
formulated this charming lit tle tarradiddle with the most
ingenious smile in the World, and she did it so well that I
Would have believed her had I not, only that morning
escorted a friend to a small, suburban frockisfs who
proudly informed us that she had just sent home Miss
Blank’s wedding-clothes ! I suppose she told me the fib
about it because she was foolishly ashamed of the hire-
ling. Now, whenever I am told the legend of that “ trous-
seau out from Home,” I simply reflect—“ Could not afford
the big shops, and got it done round the corner.” Now,
I have heard people say?—“Sappho Smith - pooh ! she’s
not a woman.” I ask you to calmly read this paragraph
and say whether I’m a woman or not?
Another instance of the illiberality of the average
clergyman occurred at a suburban church on the day of Sir
John Robertson's funeral. In the morning the organist
asked if he might play the “Dead March” in memoriam
of Sir Jack, but the head Christian of the premises
refused, in a faint and horrified voice, to allow such a
tribute to be paid to a man who had not been in the
habit, during life, of going to church three times every
Sunday. In the evenipg, hqwever, this shining example
Upended himself in his pulpit, and announced in a tearful
voice that the “ Dead March” would be played in memory
of the - aw—late Archbishop of York. So, after all, the
organist played it, and the audience took it as a tribute
to old Jack Robertson. .
The features of a very fashionable recent afternoon
musical “ at home,” were the absence of men and chairs —
two things no properly regulated woman can hope to be
without. Fancy four men and seventy women ! Also
somebody complains of the bad behaviour of two member 3
of a generally-bad-behaved family who stood under the
centre chandelier nudgingone another as each guest filled
the doorway. But the music was excellent, even if the com-
pany had to listen to it crowding round the doors and in
the hall.
Mrs. Henry Dangar’s dance, the other evening, was
poetised by a trio of debutantes all nice and fresh-looking
but lacking “go,” as usual—their simple white clothes
making them still more school-girlifled. Really girls who
are “coming-out ” ought to object to the milk-and-water
frocking forced upon them. Even the tricky belle of two
seasons couldn’t look other than meek and mild with no
padding in her frock and her shoulders tied up with baby,
ribbon. On this occasion Mrs. J. Mitchell’s fashionable
silver tinsel brocade was the most effective dress on the
floor.
Rather a costume-wedding was that of Miss Connie
Richards, of Holkharn, Randwick, to Mr. Duncan Carson,
son of Mr. John Carson, of Glutha, Studley Park, Vic-
toria, which happened Oh 10th inst., at St. Jude’s, Rand-
wick. The tall bride wore Whfte satin brocade, orange-
blossoms, Venetian point lace and chiffon, and was backed
up by three adult maids in cream serge broidered in gold
and rather theatrical hats en suite and three mites in real
Greenaway frocks to the ankles, of cream silk and
fur. At this wedding the bride innovated somewhat’by
presenting each of her three gown-maids with a lace
handkerchief. Honeymoon in Melbourne, where the
bride journeyed in electric-blue cloth braided in gold and
blue cloth hat with astrachan crown.

The Bulletin denounces the Australian
daily press as a political Thug—as a lying institu-
tion which politically has the morals of a common
garotter. Its malignant and cowardly kuppre.s-
sions and distortions 'of the utterances of the
labour-candidates dutfrig every general election
stamps it As the Cold-blooded assassin of that
“free speech ” which is its vaunted shibboleth.
Tree speech ! Tor What does mere oral utterance
count under the reign of that foul and meretricious
institution the “ independent ” Press, the
hireling and venal advocate of the rich and
the Corrupt gagger of the poor? What
avail mere spoken and unreported words,
whose direct force extends not beyond the range
of the human voice, against the übiquitous and
daily assaults and Suppressions of the omnipotent,
unsleeping, and far-reaching enemy Of the
democracy? Gentlemen, The Bulletin a‘ks you
to drink to the Australian Sup-Press—and to let
the Sup-Press have a place high up on the toast-
list of every democratic gathering.
The jewels belonging, to Her Gracious are
worth a trifle over £3,000,000; and the owner of
all this hidden wealth wears a seedy old crape
dress with a pocket in it like a coal-sack, and an
old, smashed bonnet that seems as if it had been
struck by ink.and lightning, and has no more use
for all these diamonds and rubies than she has for
5,000,< 00 briek-s. And meanwhile all this money
lies hidden and locked up, instead of being used
to pay for a good healthy revolution, and to
defray the expenses of a Wild stem-chase after the
House of Lords, with the peers bolting wildly in
front, and their bleeding country following close
behind with a fence-rail and a big rock to keep
them moving. Three millions ! Great Scott 1
What a breezy insurrection could be got up for
the money if it were only properly applied! It
would buy torpedoes enough to sink all the navies’
inexistence; and dynamite enough to scatter all
the royal families of the earth so effectually that
it would rain scraps of monarch for three weeks
afterwards; and liquor enough to make all the
standing armies of Europe oo tight that they
would bamboo their apoplectic generals and roll
the field-marshals and arch-dukes in the mud,
and .mutiny to the last man. And meanwhile it
does "no more good to anybody than a toad inside
a rock. •
With the sentiments set tbrth in the
following letter, which, as will be seen,
reached us two days before the polling. The
Bulletin concurs. It, however, refuses to believe
that the workmen as a class are.responsible for the
hideous display of blundering and cold-blooded
ingratitude which has unfortunately occurred in
connection with South Sydney election :
Dear Bulletin, —Are the tactics of the N.S.W. .Labour
party generally commendable as regards their attitude to
the Protectionist party ? Have they not wantonly and
needlessly and’illogically hampered them almost at every
turn in fighting the Parkes Government— purely an Em-
ployers’ Government—at the present crisis ?- stubbornly
refusing all overtures from .the rProtectionists, refusing to
pair with their candidates when it would have been in
the mutual interest to do so, and, above all, contesting
seats of late members who, in times of trouble, .when the
grit of public men was tested in the presence of the world,
manfully assisted them by every means in their power ?
Look at South Sydney. Was it fair to oppose
Traill, Edmunds, and Toohey, the only representatives
of- any city constituency who 'openly stood by the
workers ; members as democratic in spirit and .action
as the representatives of Northumberland in the N.S.W.
and more democratic than those of Collingwood in the
Victorian Assembly? Then, not content with bitterly
opposing these.three men, two at least of whom are so
able that they would cutmore than.respectable figures iu
any deliberative assembly, they' issue against them
grossly unfair leaflets and also workup a farcical bribery
charge against them—because the alleged “labour”
candidates, who at the present juncture look very like
unconscious, simple-minded tools of the Employers’
Union, are offered their expenses (that is what the
“ bribe ” amounts to) to retire from a contest in which
the workman is biting his brother’s nose off. At the
public meeting last Thursday, at New Masonic Hall, it
was agreed that Messrs. Higgs and Flowers should be
asked to retire. To make such a request without offering
to pay expenses incurred would have been unfair ; that
anything more was offered no one really believes.
In any case, Labour is helping Labour’s bitterest enemies
—the .men for whom the solid vote-of Property and the
Employers’ Union will go. Fancy throwing .over Traill
for Wise—that’s what may happen, and I ask, considering
the public history of these twomen, whether the exchange,,
if effected, will be one full of promise for the workers?
In view of past events, .the apparent determination erf
Labour to elect bona-fide proletarian working-men only
in all cases, is open to objection, and, as in the case of
South Sydney, means division and, perhaps, disaster—the
Eossible loss of the services of, good and tried friends of
abour. Grey, utterer of the loudest trumpet-call yet made
to Labour in this part of the world, is a born aristo-
crat—and supposing he represented South Sydney now,
would the workers try to turn him out in favour of the
unproved Higgs and Flowers, who may be either gods or
asses, and about whom the world knows, nothing, except
that they are practically cutting the throats of, the class
to which they belong ? Even Grey himself could not have
much more democratically represented South Sydney
than the present members. I feel strongly on this because
I am myself a workman, and heart and soul with the work-
men, although, to be honest, I am not a wage-earner.—
F.T., Sydney, 15th June 1891.

A Turkish Bath.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Tr , . . . .
Ajwasa slack time at a Melbourne Turkish bath,
n ,P ,°®. was that day for ladies,
and the ladies seemed slow to turn up, and the
attendant was pensively eating the top of her
pencil and thinking of nothing at all. By-and-
by° s he began to grow drowsy and in another
minute she would have fallen asleep, only a
shadow passed unexpectedly between her and the
Hunhght, and she sat up again. It was a large
shadow, and was cast by a big, sunburnt female
who was nervously standing off and on in the
doorway, and showing signals of distress. Ap-
patently she wanted to come in, and at the same
tune she wanted to stay out, and by
way of striking an average between these two
emotions she was coming in backwards, and
instead ot coming in a straight line she was rub-
bing herself along the wall. Her age was pro-
bably about 18, and she was a stout, gawky
person, evidently fresh from some untrodden
region m the bush. When she had traversed most
of the corridor she concussed against an umbrella-
stand, and would have fallen over it and landed
in great disorder on the floor, but the attendant
rushed out and caught her, and then she acknow-
ledged nervously and apologetically that she had
come to try a Turkish bath and see what it was
.
She seemed so miserably scared by her own
rashness that the bath-woman took pity on her,
and told her that her resolution was in every way
commendable. I hen she led the patient up to
the orace and waited for her to pay the accustomed
contribution. But the sufferer plainly had no
idea of what was expected of her, and tacked
back aud forward in front of the pigeon-hole, and
started to supply a brief resume of her history,
together with a quantity of interesting informa-
tion concerning her parents, and various details
regarding outside branches of the family. As she
went on it gradually dawned on the surprised
attendant that her visitor was endeavouring to
explain away the suspicious circumstance of her
wanting a bath—she wanted to show, in fact,
JOHN BURNS IN THE WORKSHOP.
[FROM A PHOTO. BY “DUSA."]
that the bath was forced upon her by circum-
stances which were too strong to be resisted—
that her whole life had led up to that bath, and
any reflections which the bath might leave upon
her character were therefore wholly undeserved.
I hen she interrupted her.
“ It’ll be three shillings, please. ”
kem out from Hingland when eleven years
of age,” pursued the customer in an agony of
nervousness, “along with the rest of the family,
two of them havin’ since died, and lived in the
country ever since down Gippsland way in aquiet,
though respectable, locality— ”
“If you’!! kindly go along with the attendant
she il show you the way.”
The attendant, in fact, was grasping her from
behind and endeavouring to conduct her along the
passage, but she was hard to move. She was
heavy, for one thing; and for another she was so
deeply involved in her efforts to clear her
character that she was quite unconscious that
anybody was shoving her. But at last she got
into sudden motion and was led in the saloon and
almost to the door of one of the little apartments
where the victim is supposed to leave her
eyery-day attire. Then she somehow backed the
distracted official against the wall, and over-
whelmed her with an observation that lasted
nearly a quarter of an hour, and covered her with
a waste of battered grammar and putrified syntax
and old disused pronunciation. At the end of
that _ time her auditor managed to shove
her into a room, containing a looking-glass,
a chair, and a bathing costume, and left her
there. After that she went out and had
hysterics in the passage, and another female had
to take her place for the rest of the day.
About three hours afterwards the lady in the
office, dropped in to make some inquiries, and by
comparing notes with the new attendant it was
discovered that a customer had got lost somewhere
on the premises. -There was a hurried investiga-
tion, and the strayed individual was found sitting
down in room No. 10. She was in an exhausted
state from want or provisions, but was sticking
manfully to her post. The leader of the search-
party went back to the office, and laughed till her
back-hair flew off, but the other rose to the
occasion. She had once been a nurse in a female
lunatic asylum, and concluding that the visitor
was a convalescent who had been ordered a
Turkish bath for the good of her intelligence, she
proceeded to undress her by force and induct her
into the bathing costume. Then she went out in
quest of a towel or something, and found the door
locked on her return, and when she hailed the
customer through the keyhole, and explained that
she was expected to come out in her scanty attire,
that inmate braced herself firmly against the door
and yelled “Thieves.” Two elderly ladies, attired
in a long white sheet apiece, who were “ cool-
ing off ” after going through the customary
process, dropped their hot coffee over each other
in terror at this sudden outbreak, and let go their
back-hair, and the magazines they were reading,
and fled in a scalded state for the door.
There was a hurried consultation of the entire
establishment, and then another attendant—the
one whose business it was to soap the visitors and
throw the hot water over them —came and nego-
tiated through the keyhole. The lady inside was
still holding the door with all her might, and had
her head braced against it and her feet against
the wall. She wanted to have her Turkish bath
inside all by herself and stated that she was born
of poor but respectable parents. Being told that
this was impossible, she refused to emerge on any
terms, and cast such reflections upon the person
outside that the latter climbed up on a chair
with a view to dragging the bather out over the
top of the partition. She came down again hur-
riedly and went away to put some cooling sub-
stance on her eye, and there was a long pause, till
a motherly old lady, who had been having a bath
to cure her rheumatism, borrowed a kitchen-table
and mounting it with difficulty, proceeded to look
over the door and remonstrate with the erring
fellow-creature on the other side. The messenger
of peace wore a large towel and a pair of spec-
tacles, and she had her hair cut short, which pos-
Si bly created a misconception on the other side,
or when the distracted girl saw her head appear
she gave a wild howl and called out “Fire!” She
said it so aggressively, too, that there was a hur-
lit u y attendants under the iiiipression
that the caged maniac was jumping at the lady on
the table and trying to seize her by the throat.
-But the tumult calmed down after this, and oil
was presently thrown upon the troubled customer
inside, ihe old lady pointed out that it was im-
possible to pass a tub of water and some soap over
the partition and that a bath, taken under such
circumstances, was cold and uncomfortable, and
moreover, it wasn’t a Turkish bath at all. Then
she assured her that ladies of the highest respecta-
bility did come out with nothing on but a bathing
costume ; and finally she gained the confidence of
her auditor, and induced her to explain the situa-
tion. It appeared that she had been reading a
romance with the beginning and the end torn out,
and a large section gone out of the centre; but in
the remains of this work it had transpired that
the heroine—whose name was Gwendoline-had a
lurlash bath on one occasion, and this fact had
led her to rush miserably into her present alarm-
ing situation. Then she broke down hopelessly
and stated .that the very thing she wanted now
was to get her clothes on and go home to her
mother, but she couldn’t get her clothes on because
she was holding the door with both hands, and if
she let go the door she was afraid the people out-
side would rush in, and carry her away to be
washed in some outlandish fashion that might
prove fatal. She added that she had been sitting
there on a chair for eight hours, and hanging on
to the door-handle for a century, and that a
strange woman had burst in and torn most of her
clothes off, and wrapped a sheet round her, and
she wished she was dead.
By this time the attendant with the damaged
eye had come back with an axe, and was hanging
round in the hope that she might have a chance to
break in the door and haul out her assailant by
violence. But she sat down in astonishment
find dropped her weapon heavily on the floor when
she saw the door open of its own accord, and the
old lady scramble down off the. table, and go in,
and embrace the rampant patient. And she was
still more surprised when the patient returned the
embrace, and buried her head in her friend’s
capacious bosom, and stated tearfully tliat she
was hungry. And finally, when the peacemaker
undertook to go and have another bath, merely in
order that she might see the forlorn stranger
safely through the ordeal, and the latter consented
to this arrangement, she went off and opened the
door quietly, and threw the axe out into the
passage. Then the old lady, in her towel and blue
spectacles, and with a kindly smile bn her coun-
tenance, stalked proudly into the “hot room,”
and her tame captive trotted after her undressed
and in her right mind.
Still the guardian angel came very near swear-
ing when shb turned round and found that her
follower was carrying all her clothes along with
her. She had her boots slung round her neck,
and a bonnet and a stocking in one hand, and the
second stocking and a whole assortment of mis-
cellaneous articles in the other, and her other
things were mostly carried in her teeth. And
when she was urgently requested to leave her
clothes in the dressing-room she responded with
the unanswerable conundrum: “Supposin’ they
was to be took ?”
“ But they won’t be taken, my child. Nobody
will touch them. ”
“But if they tvas to be took, I couldn’t get
home like this.”
This was undeniable, but the old lady settled
the matter by taking firm hold of the whole con-
signment of clothes and carrying them out. When
she opened the door in coming back she concussed
it heavily against the head of her protege, who was
following her movements through the keyhole.
That miserable sufferer had been seized with an
instantaneous conviction that her supposed friend
had absconded with the wearing apparel, and that
she was left behind—a lost girl in a bathing cos-
tume adrift on the hard surface of a strange land.
She was beginning to puff heavily in the heated
atmosphere, but still she looked unspeakably
relieved when she found that her suspicions were
baseless. After that she endured in silence for
about five minutes awful perspiration, and at
the end of that period began to pray for air.
guardian led her over to the fountain and
told her to stoop down.
She stooped.
Then the old lady emptied about a quart of cold
water over her head, and asked her if she felt
better.
The girl returned with surprising suddenness to
an erect position and answered with a glare. She
made no reply in words, but her eye expressed
the indignation of a female who had lost all faith
in the human race. It said, as plainly as a whole
dictionary of vituperation, that she had trusted
this perfidious old woman, and the old woman
had betrayed her and surreptitiously thrown cold
water on her head. It stated, as distinctly as
could state, that all was over between
them. It was the sort of look that Ctesar might
have worn if he had found himself unexpectedly
washed by Brutus in the Forum—the look that
Cato might have assumed if the whole Roman
republic had been treacherously shampooed by an
enemy in his absence. Then she solemnly walked
back to the dressing-room and proceeded to get
into her clothes with all possible haste, and fly
from that scene of baseness and deception. And
when the old lady, in a spirit of Christian for-
bearance, appealed to her through the crack to
come out with her and have some refreshment
she only banged indignantly on the other side of
the door with her boots, but other response there
was none. The proud spirit of the daughter of
the wilderness was wounded beyond all repair.

Who Shall Show Us any Good?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Brother-worker, weather-beaten, weary-worn with
grinding labour,
Plodding on, ass-like, contented, with the world
upon your back ;
Sourly solaced that you really fare no harder than
your neighbour ;
Grimly happy when possessing comforts that
your fellows lack;
Self-abasing in the presence of a proudling ; little
thinking ,
That among the social orders you're a member
of the chief,
For With all their class distinctions; "tis a truth
will bear no blinking :
He who is not of the workers is a beggar or a
thief.
Never seek you greater guerdon than to live and
bear your burden ?
Slave too base to feel your fetters, would you
fancy you are free ?
Does the hard fact ne'er concern you, while you
lick the feet that spurn you,
That the drones do thrive and fatten on the
produce of the bee?
What to you are all the triumphs, all the glorious
gains of science ?
You, “the heir of all the ages, 55 what does
birthright mean for you ?
While the parrot-cry of “Progress 55 you repeat
with blind compliance,
Still the hard-got fruits of labour are the plunder
of the few.
Who shall show us any good? You grave and
venerable sages—•
Doctors of a musty learning, by the world
accounted wise—
While you sit amid the twilight pouring over
faded pages,
Find you not the breaking dawn too dazzling
for your owlish eyes ?
Shall we look to you for leading in the Day of Fate
approaching ?
You who waste your whole lives pondering over
riddles of the past !
Though the world of men is ever some new mine
of knowledge broaching,
Each successive age evolves a sphinx niore subtle
than the last.
Who shall show us any good ? You clever men Of
words and phrases
Weaving flimsy threads of thought into fantastic
filigree !
All your conjuring with letters merely mystifies
and dazes.
We are weary of your babble and your verbal
jugglery.
Is Hot all the great world humming with the stir
of waking nations?
Whs there ever theme more fit to set the singer's
soul aflame ?
Truly he who gives a voice, to man's most noble
aspirations
Best fulfils the poet's part and takes the royal
road to fame.
Shall we look to you for good, you smug and
shallow-hearted preacher ?
You, whose sermons lead to notlfing or some far-
fetched meaning fit
On to words of truth once spoken by the ages'
truest Teacher,
Lest they vex the ear of Dives, at whose board
you love to sit.
Can you look with cool complaisance on a world
of wealth and hunger,
Deeming it divinely ordered to continue
through all time?
“Poor ye always have among you.” Go, blas-
pheming sennon-m nger,
Tis the Christ you're crucifying; go, your cant
is foulest crime.
Statesman, you with brains so weighty, often over-
racked and heated,
Solving problems all too simple to be subtly
understood;
Impotent to cope with evils in a rotten system
seated,
You would blindly set them down as needful
for the common good ;
Holding him who serves the masses guilty of the
grossest treason ;
Ever sacredly regarding vested rights built up
on wrong;
Reared on scaly usage scarcely savoured with the
salt of reason ;
Hidebound stag, your place is wanted; you have
blocked our path too long.
Who shall show us any good ? Peace ! Hear you
not the solemn presage ?
Hear you not a voice prophetic crying in the
wilderness ?
Lo! 'tis he, the great forerunner bearing unto man
the message
To prepare the way of him who comes to bring
the world redress.
Be of courage, then, O brother, striving for the
life ideal ;
Dare assert your manhood's right though cravens
croak and cynics sneer,
What to-day is called a dream shall soon become
a thing that’s real,
And the days of dearth shall pass and then
shall come the Golden Year.
TOM FREEMAN.
27 Ross-street, Richmond, Vic.
Dear Bulletin, —Her Gracious is still
“ very much grieved when an accident happens in
amine.” The other day a miner was killed at
Ballarat (Vic.), and Hopetoun, as her Gracious 5
deputy, was all broken Tip with compassion.
Therefore he telegraphed to the Mayor, and —to
quote the eloquent words of the local paper—
“the mayor called on Mrs. Walters, who is in
very poor circumstances, and conveyed to her the
sympathy of the Governor. 55 Sympathy of that
kind ought to be suppressed by Act of Parlia-
ment. When a family has lost its breadwinner
and is left lonely and destitute, it is an outrage
on all good taste for a complete stranger to tramp
in merely to mention that Hopetoun is feeling
sorry, and then tramp out again, and Hopetoun 5 s
grief isn 5 t a large enough substance to justify the
intrusion. Moreover, Hopetoun isn't grieved,
except in his official capacity. There isn't room
for more than a pint of grief in any average man,
and a Governor can 5 t hold more woe than other
people ; and when he comes to a strange country,
and proceeds to slop over with a whole quart of
sympathy whenever a man he never saw falls
down a shaft, the sentiment is too copious to be
real. If a Governor is so badly hurt about the
death of a complete stranger, he must have felt
dreadful about the innards when he was at home
and his own personal acquaintances took to
dying ; in fact, to judge by his present condition,
it seems reasonable to suppose that he would
have been so badly broken up that he couldn't
get out to Australia at all, and yet the average
Gov. arrives in good condition, and looking quite
cheerful. Further, I have personally inspected a
Governor less than an hour after he had tele-
graphed a whole cargo of unmitigated woe about
a big railway-accident, and he seemed absolutely
jovial; and I have inspected the same Governor
at a banquet, and watched the haggard expression
in his dejected eye as he got up and stated that it
gave him the greatest pleasure to be present; and
I have come to the conclusion that the conven-
tional viceroy enjoys his grief, and feels darned
miserable over his happiness. Under these cir-
cumstances it would be a great deal more accurate
if the imported Governor sent a £2O note and left
the grief out altogether; if he must send some-
thing it might as well be something that would
look more probable than the thing he has sent
hitherto.—Yours, as usual, Titus Salt.

“ A Crolclen Shanty*”
PRICE-ONE SHILLING.
Various Verses.
The Sheoak's Song.
In merry golden days of youth;
Do I remember well,
t wandered by a, mountain creek,
Where dancing waters fell.
There stood a row of sheoaks there,
. And picturesque were they,
And, in their shade, a maiden fair,' 5 !
With whom I talked one day ;
While, from the hill above our heads,
, The lyre-bird’s call we heard,
And watched the yellow waters flow,
By busy diggers stirred.
Far up the vale, we saw tjieir tents
Gleam white amid the trees,
And heard the stroke of pick or axe,
Upon the wandering breeze ;
hut through it all, and o’er it all,
With sway, and sigh, and swell,
The murmur in the sheoak trees,
Like music, rose and fell.
She raised her hand and"said to m
, “ Hark to the branches’ song,
And mark the tone of sorrow now
That breathes the leaves among;
As if some gentle forest sprite
Roamed from the woods apart,
And heard a tale of lonely grief
From some poor broken heart,
And brought it here and told it o
The solemn bush around,
To show them of the misery
In hunian dwellings found.”
I laughed, and said, “ No tores t fay
Wears such woeladen wings;
’Tis but a soothing lullaby
Old Mother Nature sings ;
Only a lazy song she has,
To croon a man to rest,
To lie upon this grassy bank,
And sleep upon her breast,
besides, a tale ot woe, 1 fear,
Would here unheeded fall,
When, all too oft, the human ear
Is deaf to' pity’s call.”
< i . . • • >
I stand again beside the creek,
And hear the wild birds trill,
And see the long grass waving brown,
Along the sifnny hill
But the sheoaks’ line is broken,
The axe has laid them prone,
The creek runs crystal o’er its sands.
The diggers’ tents are gone ;
And I am old, and altered, too,
While She, who spoke that day,
lies low within a grassy grave,
A hundred miles away.
But o’er my head, one sheoak stands,
And murmurs, soft and low,
The same old, endless, changing song
It sang so long ago
But, ah, its voice has changed since then*,
The lone and whispering tree
Tells now of peace and rest for her
And sadness unto me ;
But soon, oh soon, old Mother Earth
Shall take me to her breast;
And kindly Death shall come, and turn'
My sorrow into rest. '•
GEO. S. COOK.
Durante Vita.
Life to all youth is golden and red,.
And radiant with richness of light;,
Naught is known of the storm-clouds ahead,
Nor the gladness of life that is withered and dead,,
For all is so fair to the sight.
The blood-writ sorrows are never known,.
Nor wearying efforts, nor tears,
N.or endless hope, nor the dying moan.
Nor the living forsaken pining alone, ,
That pace with the gathering years.
The future betrays not the sorrows hid
In its mirage distant, unreal,
Still mankind ever will revel amid—
The children alike as their fathers did—
The myst’ries it may reveal.
But in that future, silent and stern,
Of which Time carries the key,
Youth sees naught but the pleasures that burn,,
Only the sheen of the dazzling morn -
Aflame on a silvery sea.
The glad wind rushes and dallies along
With a perfume rare on its breath,
And nothing wearies and naught is wrong,,
And joy is the burden of every song,
Though tire songs are sad as death..
Bp, age is here, and but dimly seen.
Are the glories passed away,
Glory of youth and of sunset sheen—
Darkness and death where light had been,
How cheerless and still and grey !
Ah me, but the fairest lives will drift
To a saddened sombre shore !
Sooner or later the veil will lift,
And we with our gladness and tears will shift;
And why should we wish for more ?
H. E. P. M‘DONALD.
Brisbane.
Vindication.
They told me lies who said your eyes
(That gleam with love whene’er I woo thee) ,
Are false, as thou art fair, my love,
’Tis only true of one, my beauty.
They said you were a hollow sham,
A hollow sham,, less saint than sinner ;:
The cowards lied, my bonnie lamb,
’Twas false —at least just after dinner.
You’d flirted oft, they said, with men ;
The lie was just a truth inverted,
And thus I turn it back again—
’Tis many men with you have flirted.,
Ah, love, my love, heed not their jeers,
No matter though the whole world flout thee,
In spite of all their scoffs and sneers
I never can think worse about thee.
Sydney.
E.J.D.

In the Droving Days.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Only a pound ! ” said the auctioneer,
“ Only a pomid ! And I’m standing here
Selling this animal, gain or loss,
Only a pound for the drover’s horse !
Oile of the sort that was ne’er afraid;
One of the Boys df the Old Brigade,
Thoroughly honest and game, I’ll swear;
Only a little the worse for wear—
Plenty as bad to be seen in towil,
Give me a bid and I’ll knock him down !
Sdld as he stands ! And without recour.:o !
Give nie a bid for the drover’s horse ! ”
Loitering there irt an aimless way
I cast one look at the poor old gray ;
Weary and battered and screwed, of cour.rc;
But when I sighted the old gray horse,
The rough bush saddle, and single rein
Of the bridle laid on his tangled mane;
Somehow the crowd and the auctioneer
Seemed on a sudden to disappear—
Melted away in a kind of haze,
For my heart went back tp the droving days,
And in my dreaming I crossed again
The miles and miles of the saltbush plain,
The shining plain that is said to be
The dried-up bed of an inland sea,
Where the air so dry and so clear and bright
Refracts the sun with a wondrous light,
And out on the dim horizon makes
The deep, blue gleam of tire phantom lakes.
At the dawn of day we would feel the breeze
As it stirred the boughs of the sleeping trees,
And brought a breath of the fragrance rare
That comes and goes in that scented air,
For trees and grass and shrubs contain
A dry, sweet scent on the saltbush plain.
For those that know it and understand
The saltbush plain is a wonder land,
A wondrous country where Nature’s ways
Were revealed to rpe in the droving days.
Ah me ! how quickly they crowded back,
The mem’ries old of the drover’s track.
We heard the chime that the bell-birds ring,
And the glorious song that the magpies sing.
At mid-day camps in the sultry noon
We would watch the swans in the still lagoon,
Or through the clearings we saw by chance
The wild companion’s stately dance.
We watch’d the fleet wild horses pass,
And the kangaroos through the Mitchell grass ;
The emu ran with her frightened brood—
All unmolested and unpursued.
But there rose a shout and a wild hubbub
When the dingo raced for his native scrub,
And he paid right dear for his stolen meals,
With the drover’s dogs at his wretched heels ;
For we ran him down at a rattling pace ;
The pack-horse joined in the stirring chase,
And a wild halloo at the death we'd raise
We were light of heart in the droving days.
’Twas a drover’s horse ! And my hand again
Made a move to close on a fancied rein ;
For I felt the swing and the easy stride
Of a grand old horse that I used to ride.
In drought or plenty, in good or ill,
That same old steed was my comrade still ;
We worked together at dawn of light,
And camped together at fall of night :
For the old gray horse with his honest ways
Was a mate to me in the droving days.
Many a time when the nights were damp,
And the cattle rushed from the sleeping-camp
In the Mulga scrub and across the plain,
With my head bent down on his waving mane.
Through the boughs above and the stumps
below,
In the darkest night I could let him go.
At a racing speed he would choose his course,
And my life was safe with the old gray horse ;
And never a once would he cry a crack
Till he caught the cattle and wheeled them
back.
But man and horse had a fav’rite job
When an outlaw broke from a station mob ;
With a right good will was the stockwhip plied
As the old horse raced at the straggler’s side,
And the green-hide thong such a weal would
raise
We could use the whip in the droving days.
Only a pound ! And was this the end ?
Only a pound for the drover’s friend,
The drover’s friend that had seen his day
And now was worthless and cast away,
With a broken knee, and a broken heart
To be flogged and starved in a hawker’s cart!
Well, I made a bid from a sense of shame,
And the memories sake of the good old game.
“ Thank you ! Guinea ! And cheap at that !
Against you there in the curly hat !
Only a guinea ! and one more chance,
Down he goes if there’s no advance.
Third and the last time, one ! two ! three ! ”
And the old gray horse was knocked down to
me.
And now he’s wandering fat and sleek
On the lucerne flats by the homestead creek :
I dare not mount him for fear he’d fall,
But he does a journey to beat them all ;
For though he scarcely a trot can raise
He can take me back to the droving days.

Melbourne people express some sympathy
for Mr. M. J. Jones, one of the ex-committee of
the Premier Building Association, who stands
practically alone in defending a suit for the
recovery of £'(5003 deposited with the society at a
time when the limit of its loan and deposit re-
ceipts had, as the plaintiff alleges, been already
exceeded. Mr. Jones turned up his billet on the
committee after a very brief experience of the
general rottenness of things, and was not included
in the famous conspiracy charge. In the present
case he comes in amongst a group of seven defend-
ants, but as the other six are either luxuriating in
Europe, or hanging on to their shattered fortunes
with nothing ranch to lose, *' the action is mainly
directed against J ones. ” He seems to be regarded
as the only available person worth going for, and
the crying shame of permitting “the Age’s blood
relation to skedaddle is freely anathematised.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,’*
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Governments
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The BlT.leTin denounces —
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Im peri al Fed era t ion.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and uas become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the,countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
hav e unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the. fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aeainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must-indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kuqblmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months 1 was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1883.
Peter Smvthe, J.P,
